For E, X(t) on Ω: |X(t) - M|^2 = r^2. M=(0,0). So (x_A + t v_x)^2 + (y_A + t v_y)^2 = r^2. Since A satisfies x_A^2 + y_A^2 = r^2, we have:

(x_A^2 + y_A^2) + 2t (x_A v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = r^2 => 2t (x_A v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0. So t=0 or t = -2 (x_A v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2. Thus t_E = -2 (x_A v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2.

Similarly, for F, X(t) on Γ: |X(t) - N|^2 = R^2. N=(d,0). So (x_A + t v_x - d)^2 + (y_A + t v_y)^2 = R^2. A satisfies (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2. Expand: (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 + 2t ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 => 2t ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0. So t=0 or t = -2 ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2. Thus t_F = -2 ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2.

Thus we have E = X(t_E), F = X(t_F). So coordinates expressed.

Now we need circle through B, E, F. B = (x_A, -y_A). Since E and F are on line AP, we might find some properties: maybe B is the reflection of A across line MN? Actually B = (x_A, -y_A). So B is symmetric of A across x-axis (MN). Since M and N are on x-axis, the circles are symmetric across x-axis. So B is the other intersection of the circles. So B lies on both circles as well. So B is on Ω and Γ. So we have A and B symmetric about MN. So line AP is some line through A; its second intersection with Ω is E, with Γ is F. So B is also on both circles; maybe B, E, F are related by some symmetry.

Let's try to compute using coordinates to see if we can prove tangency. Since we have explicit coordinates for A, P, H, and we can compute E and F in terms of A and P, then find circle (BEF) and verify that ℓ is tangent. This is algebraic but we can try to simplify using known relations.

Let’s define coordinates using parameters: d, r, R. But we also have x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d), y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). That's messy because of square root. However, we can use that y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2, and also we have relation with R: (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2. So we can treat x_A and y_A as variables satisfying these equations. Instead of square root, we can treat y_A as a positive number but keep y_A^2.

We have p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Also we computed p_y = - (x_A + r)(d+R - r)/(2 y_A). So that's expressed.

Now v = P - A = (p_x - x_A, p_y - y_A). Compute p_x - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - x_A = (d+R-r - 2x_A)/2. We earlier computed p_x - x_A = (R - r)(d+R)/(2d). Let's verify: (R - r)(d+R)/(2d) = (R - r)(d+R)/(2d). So that is p_x - x_A. Good.

p_y - y_A = - (x_A + r)(d+R - r)/(2 y_A) - y_A = -[ (x_A + r)(d+R - r) + 2 y_A^2 ]/(2 y_A). Expand: x_A(d+R-r) + r(d+R-r) + 2r^2 - 2x_A^2 = x_A(d+R-r) - 2x_A^2 + r(d+R-r) + 2r^2. That's not obviously simplified. We have: t_E = -2 (x_A v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2. But note that v_x = p_x - x_A, v_y = p_y - y_A. So x_A v_x + y_A v_y = x_A(p_x - x_A) + y_A(p_y - y_A) = x_A p_x - x_A^2 + y_A p_y - y_A^2 = x_A p_x + y_A p_y - (x_A^2 + y_A^2) = x_A p_x + y_A p_y - r^2. So t_E = -2 (x_A p_x + y_A p_y - r^2)/|v|^2.

Similarly, for t_F: ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y) = (x_A - d)(p_x - x_A) + y_A(p_y - y_A) = (x_A - d)p_x - (x_A - d)x_A + y_A p_y - y_A^2 = (x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - (x_A(x_A - d) + y_A^2) = (x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - (x_A^2 - x_A d + y_A^2) = (x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - (r^2 - x_A d) = (x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - r^2 + x_A d. So t_F = -2 ((x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - r^2 + x_A d)/|v|^2 = -2 ((x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - r^2 + x_A d)/|v|^2.

Given the complexity, maybe there is a known geometry result: In a configuration of two intersecting circles, the line through the orthocenter of triangle formed by the centers and the circumcenter of a certain triangle parallel to the line joining an intersection point and that circumcenter is tangent to the circumcircle of the other intersection and the two points where that line meets the circles again. This might be a known lemma from IMO Shortlist or something.

We can attempt to prove the tangency condition using vectors or coordinates and simplify using algebraic identities. Let's proceed with analytic geometry using complex numbers with M=0, N=1, and treat points as complex numbers. We'll denote:

Let a = R - r (distance between radii), b = R + r. Then conditions: 0 < a < 1 < b, and r = (b-a)/2, R = (b+a)/2. But we also have that circles intersect: a < 1 < b.

Define:
- M = 0, N = 1.
- A is intersection of circles |z|=r and |z-1|=R. Since we choose upper intersection, we have A = x_A + i y_A with y_A > 0.
- Then B = conjugate of A = x_A - i y_A.
- C = -r (since C is on Ω on the ray from M away from N, so on negative real axis, distance r from M: C = -r).
- D = 1+R (since D is on Γ on ray from N away from M, so on positive real axis, distance R from N: D = 1+R).
- P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are real, P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD: x = (C+D)/2 = (-r + 1+R)/2 = (1+R-r)/2 = (1 + (R-r))/2 = (1+a)/2. So p_x = (1+a)/2. And p_y = - (x_A + r)(1+R-r)/(2 y_A) as derived. So P = p_x + i p_y, with p_y < 0.
- H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=0, N=1 are real, the altitude from P is vertical (perpendicular to MN which is horizontal), so H = (p_x, h_y) with h_y = p_x (1-p_x)/p_y (since p_y negative, h_y negative). So H = p_x + i h_y, with h_y = p_x (1-p_x)/p_y.
- Line AP: direction = A - P = (x_A - p_x) + i (y_A - p_y). Since p_y negative, y_A - p_y > 0.
- Points E and F: E is second intersection of line AP with Ω (|z|=r). Since A is on Ω, and line AP goes through P, we have by power of point P with respect to Ω: (PA)*(PE) = |P|^2 - r^2 (signed product). But careful with orientation: If we consider directed distances from P along line AP, then the product of the signed distances from P to A and P to E equals the power. Since we can treat complex numbers, we can find E = P + t_E (A-P) where t_E is a real number (not 0) such that |E|^2 = r^2. Using earlier parameterization: let u = A - P. Then points X = P + λ u. Now we need to show that line through H with direction u = A-P is tangent to circle (BEF). Since we have explicit expressions, we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then check the tangency condition. This is a purely algebraic task; we can attempt to simplify using the fact that y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2 and also (x_A-1)^2+y_A^2=R^2, so we have relations: x_A = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). So we can treat x_A and y_A as variables satisfying x_A^2+y_A^2=r^2 and (x_A-1)^2+y_A^2=R^2, and also r<R. Then we can compute everything in terms of x_A,y_A,r,R. However, we also have p_x = (1+R-r)/2, which is expressed in r,R, but r is given by distance from M to C, and C is at -r, so r =? Actually r is the radius of Ω, which is also the distance from M to A, so r = sqrt(x_A^2+y_A^2). That's consistent.

So we can treat x_A and y_A as coordinates satisfying x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2 and (x_A-1)^2+y_A^2 = R^2. From these, we can derive x_A = (1+r^2-R^2)/2. So x_A is expressed in terms of r,R, and y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). So the only free parameters are r and R, with the condition that the circles intersect: |R-r| < 1 < R+r. Since r<R, we have R-r < 1 < R+r. So R < 1+r and R > 1-r. Also r>0, R>r.

Thus we have a two-parameter family (r,R) satisfying R∈(max(r,1-r), 1+r). So we can treat r,R as parameters. Then we can compute everything in terms of r,R, and then the tangency condition should hold identically. So we can attempt to compute the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to line ℓ, and the circumradius, and show equality. But computing O and radius might be messy.

Alternatively, we can use the condition that the line L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the power of H with respect to the circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the circle? Actually no: For a point H, the line through H with direction u is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the distance from the center O to the line equals the radius. That is equivalent to that the foot of perpendicular from O to line L lies on the circle, and the line is perpendicular to radius. But we can also compute the equation of the circle and the line, and set the discriminant of the intersection quadratic to zero. That might be simpler: The circle (BEF) can be represented by its equation in coordinates; then plug in the parametric line H + t u, and require that the quadratic in t has a double root. This gives an equation that should hold identically in parameters. We can try to compute that using symbolic algebra, but we need to do it manually with clever simplifications.

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a synthetic approach that is more elegant. Let's try to understand the configuration more deeply.

We have two circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. The line MN (centers) meets circles at C (on Ω) and D (on Γ). So C and D are the endpoints of the diameters? Not necessarily, but they are the points where the line through centers meets the circles. So C and D are the "outer" points relative to the intersection? Since M is between C and N, and N between M and D, we have C-M-N-D. So C is on the left side of M, D on the right side of N. So the segment CD contains M and N. So C and D are on opposite sides of the segment MN? Actually they are on the same line, with C left of M, D right of N. So the circles are on opposite sides of the centers? 

Now triangle ACD: points A (intersection), C (on Ω left), D (on Γ right). Its circumcenter P. Since A is on both circles, and C and D are on the circles, there is a relation: The circles Ω and Γ are the circles with centers M,N passing through A and C (for Ω) and A and D (for Γ). So the circle (ACD) is the circumcircle of A, C, D. This circle might be orthogonal to something? 

Consider the power of M with respect to circle (ACD): Since M is center of Ω, and C lies on Ω, we have MC=r. Also, A lies on Ω, so MA=r. So M is equidistant to A and C, so M lies on perpendicular bisector of AC. But does that imply that M lies on the circumcircle of ACD? No. However, note that P is the circumcenter, so PA=PC=PD. So P is the center of circle through A,C,D. So M is such that MA=MC, so M is on perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, N is such that NA=ND, so N is on perpendicular bisector of AD. So M and N are on perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD respectively. So the triangle PMN: P is circumcenter of ACD, M is on perpendicular bisector of AC, N on perpendicular bisector of AD. So maybe triangle PMN is related to the triangle formed by the circumcenters of some triangles.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So PH is altitude to MN (horizontal). So H lies on vertical line through P. So H = (p_x, h_y) as before.

Now line through H parallel to AP: since AP is a line through A and P, and H is vertically below P (since h_y negative, p_y negative), the line through H parallel to AP is essentially the translation of AP by the vector from P to H. That is, if we take line AP and shift it so that P moves to H, we get a line through H parallel to AP. So L = AP + (H-P). So L is the line obtained by translating AP by vector v = H-P. Since H-P = (0, h_y - p_y) = (0, (h_y - p_y)). So L is the vertical translation of AP by some amount.

Now, we want to show that L is tangent to circle (BEF). Since E and F are on AP, the circle (BEF) passes through B and the two points on AP. So perhaps L is the tangent at B to circle (BEF)? That would require that L is perpendicular to radius at B, but B is not on L generally. Or maybe L is the tangent at some point on the circle that is the reflection of something.

Another possibility: Use the concept of "Miquel point" of quadrilateral formed by lines? Consider quadrilateral formed by lines AB, AC, AD, and something? Not sure.

Given the difficulty, I think an analytic solution is feasible if we choose a coordinate system that simplifies P and H. For instance, we can set the midpoint of CD as origin, and also set the line MN as x-axis. Then C = (-c,0), D = (d,0). But we also have that M and N lie on x-axis between C and D. So M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with -c < m < n < d. And we have r = m+c, R = d-n. Then we can also set the circle (ACD) with center P = ( (d-c)/2, p_y ). And we have that A satisfies |A-M|=r, |A-N|=R. So we can express A in terms of m,n,r,R. But we also have that P is the circumcenter of ACD, so PA = PC = PD. So we can compute p_y from the condition that PA = PC. That gives:

|A - P|^2 = |C - P|^2. Since C = (-c,0), P = (p_x,0) with p_x = (d-c)/2? Actually careful: If we set midpoint of CD as origin, then C = -c, D = d, so midpoint is (d-c)/2? Wait, if we set origin at the midpoint of CD, then C = -c, D = c? No, that would be symmetric. But we want the midpoint of CD to be origin, so we set C = -k, D = k for some k. Then the midpoint is 0. But then the line MN is still the x-axis, and M,N are between C and D. So we can set C = -c, D = c, with c>0. Then the midpoint is 0. Then p_x = 0 (since perpendicular bisector of CD is y-axis). So P = (0, p_y). That's a nice simplification: If we set the midpoint of CD as origin, then P lies on the y-axis. So p_x=0. This is valid because we have freedom to translate horizontally so that the midpoint of CD is at origin. So let's do that: Let the midpoint of CD be the origin. Then C = -c (real), D = c (real) for some c>0. Then the line MN is the x-axis (since it's the line through centers, we can set it as x-axis). Then M and N are points on x-axis between C and D, so -c < M < N < c. Let M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with m,n real and -c < m < n < c. Then we have that C lies on Ω: distance from M to C is r, so |m - (-c)| = m+c = r (since m>-c, m+c>0). So r = m+c. Similarly, D lies on Γ: distance from N to D is R, so |n-c| = c-n = R (since n<c). So R = c-n. So we have:

m = r - c, n = c - R.

Also, note that M and N are between C and D: -c < m < n < c, so r-c > -c => r>0, and c-R > -c => R<2c? Actually n>m implies c-R > r-c => 2c > r+R. So we have r+R < 2c.

Now A is an intersection of circles with centers M,N and radii r,R. So A satisfies |A-M|=r, |A-N|=R. Also, A is not on the x-axis (since circles intersect at two points, one above, one below). We can denote A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A>0. Then we have:

(x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2, (x_A - n)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2.

Subtracting: (x_A - m)^2 - (x_A - n)^2 = r^2 - R^2 => (x_A^2 -2mx_A+m^2) - (x_A^2 -2nx_A+n^2) = -2m x_A + m^2 +2n x_A - n^2 = 2x_A(n-m) + (m^2-n^2) = 2x_A(n-m) + (m-n)(m+n) = (n-m)(2x_A - (m+n)). So (n-m)(2x_A - (m+n)) = r^2 - R^2. Since n>m, we have 2x_A - (m+n) = (r^2 - R^2)/(n-m). But we can also find x_A and y_A directly.

Also, note that the circumcenter of triangle ACD: C=(-c,0), D=(c,0), A=(x_A,y_A). Since C and D are symmetric about y-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is the y-axis (x=0). So P lies on y-axis: P = (0, p_y). And we have PA = PC = PD. So we can compute p_y from condition PA = PC:

PA^2 = (x_A - 0)^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2 = x_A^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2.
PC^2 = ( -c - 0 )^2 + (0 - p_y)^2 = c^2 + p_y^2.
Set equal: x_A^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2 = c^2 + p_y^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 - 2y_A p_y + p_y^2 = c^2 + p_y^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 - 2y_A p_y = c^2 => 2y_A p_y = x_A^2 + y_A^2 - c^2. But note that x_A^2 + y_A^2 =? Not directly r^2? Actually from circle Ω: (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => x_A^2 -2m x_A + m^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 + 2m x_A - m^2. But we can compute p_y = (x_A^2 + y_A^2 - c^2)/(2y_A). That is similar to earlier.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=(m,0), N=(n,0), P=(0,p_y). And note that m and n are not necessarily symmetric about 0. The line MN is x-axis (horizontal). Actually from our earlier expression, p_y = (x_A^2+y_A^2-c^2)/(2y_A). Not sure sign. But we can keep it.

Thus H = (0, - m n / p_y). Note that m and n are between -c and c; m could be negative, n positive? Possibly. Since order C-M-N-D: C=-c, M=m, N=n, D=c. So m could be negative if M is left of origin, but origin is midpoint of CD, and M is between C and N; C=-c, so M could be negative or positive. Similarly, N could be positive or negative. However, typically M and N are on opposite sides of the midpoint? Not necessarily. But we can assume without loss that M is left of N, and both between -c and c. The origin might be between M and N? Not forced. But we can treat m,n as real numbers.

Now line through H parallel to AP: AP is line through A=(x_A,y_A) and P=(0,p_y). So direction vector u = A - P = (x_A, y_A - p_y). Now B is the other intersection of Ω and Γ: since A and B are symmetric about MN (x-axis), B = (x_A, -y_A). (Because reflection across x-axis). So B = (x_A, -y_A).

Points E and F: second intersections of line AP with circles Ω and Γ. Since A is on both, and line AP passes through P, we can compute E and F using power. As before, let u = A-P. Then for any point X = P + λ u, condition to be on Ω: |X - M|^2 = r^2. Since A corresponds to λ=1, we have the other root λ_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. Similarly, λ_F = (|P-N|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. So E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u.

Now, note that M and N are on x-axis, and P is on y-axis. So we can compute |P-M|^2 = (0-m)^2 + (p_y-0)^2 = m^2 + p_y^2. Similarly, |P-N|^2 = n^2 + p_y^2. So λ_E = (m^2+p_y^2 - r^2)/|u|^2, λ_F = (n^2+p_y^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. But we also have that r = m+c, and R = c-n. So m^2+p_y^2 - r^2 = m^2+p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 = m^2+p_y^2 - m^2 -2mc - c^2 = p_y^2 -2mc - c^2. Similarly, n^2+p_y^2 - R^2 = n^2+p_y^2 - (c-n)^2 = n^2+p_y^2 - (c^2 -2cn + n^2) = p_y^2 - c^2 + 2cn. So λ_E = (p_y^2 -2mc - c^2)/|u|^2, λ_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2cn)/|u|^2.

Now also note that we have relation from A being on Ω: |A-M|^2 = r^2. That gives (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = (m+c)^2. Expand: x_A^2 -2m x_A + m^2 + y_A^2 = m^2 + 2mc + c^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 - 2m x_A = 2mc + c^2. So x_A^2+y_A^2 = 2m x_A + 2mc + c^2 = 2m(x_A + c) + c^2. Now u = A-P = (x_A, y_A - p_y) = (c(2m+1), y_A - m(2c(m+1))/y_A). Since p_y = m (x_A+c)/y_A = m * 2c(m+1)/y_A = 2c m (m+1)/y_A. So y_A - p_y = y_A - 2c m (m+1)/y_A = (y_A^2 - 2c m (m+1))/y_A.

Now compute y_A^2 - 2c m (m+1) =? Using earlier: y_A^2 = 4c m(m+1) + c^2 - c^2 (2m+1)^2. So y_A^2 - 2c m(m+1) = 4c m(m+1) + c^2 - c^2 (2m+1)^2 - 2c m(m+1) = 2c m(m+1) + c^2 - c^2 (2m+1)^2. So y_A - p_y = (2c m(m+1) + c^2 - c^2 (2m+1)^2)/y_A.

Thus u = (c(2m+1), (2c m(m+1) + c^2 - c^2 (2m+1)^2)/y_A).

Now also note that we have the relation for A on Ω: (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = (m+c)^2. This gave us x_A = c(2m+1). Let's verify that with our expressions: (x_A - m)^2 = (c(2m+1)-m)^2 = (2c m + c - m)^2 = ((2c-1)m + c)^2. Not obviously equal to something. But we derived x_A from the circle equations; it should hold.

Now we have λ_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. But p_y^2 = (2c m(m+1)/y_A)^2 = 4c^2 m^2 (m+1)^2 / y_A^2. And 2mc + c^2 = c(2m+c). So p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2 = 4c^2 m^2 (m+1)^2 / y_A^2 - c(2m+c). That seems messy.

Maybe we can compute λ_E using the power of P with respect to Ω: Pow_Ω(P) = |P-M|^2 - r^2. But we also have that |P-M|^2 = m^2 + p_y^2. And r = m+c. So Pow_Ω(P) = m^2 + p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 = p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2. So that's λ_E |u|^2. So λ_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2.

Similarly, λ_F = (n^2 + p_y^2 - R^2)/|u|^2, with n = m+1, R = c-n = c-(m+1). So n^2 + p_y^2 - R^2 = (m+1)^2 + p_y^2 - (c-(m+1))^2 = (m+1)^2 + p_y^2 - (c^2 -2c(m+1)+(m+1)^2) = p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1). So λ_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2.

Now note that p_y = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. So p_y^2 = 4c^2 m^2 (m+1)^2 / y_A^2. And also we have y_A^2 expressed. This might lead to cancellations.

Perhaps we can compute |u|^2 = x_A^2 + (y_A-p_y)^2. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for u by noting that u = (x_A, y_A-p_y) is the vector from P to A. Since P is on y-axis, we can also compute the slope of AP: (y_A - p_y)/x_A. That slope is m_AP.

Not exactly because C and D are on x-axis and A is above, but B is below. However, note that the circle (ACD) is centered at P. Its reflection across x-axis is circle through B, C, D centered at P'. So that circle is symmetric. Now, points E and F are on AP. Their reflections across x-axis are on BP'. So the circle (BEF) might be related to the circle (A E' F') which is the reflection of (BEF). Then E = P + λ_E u = (0,-0.566) + (-0.6759)*(1.2,1.697) = ( -0.8111, -0.566 -1.147) = (-0.8111, -1.713). So E ≈ (-0.811, -1.713). λ_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2? Actually we can compute using the power of P with respect to Γ. n = m+1 = 0.8, R = c-n = 2-0.8=1.2, so R^2=1.44. |P-N|^2 = (0-0.8)^2 + (-0.566)^2 = 0.64+0.320=0.96. So Pow_Γ(P) = 0.96-1.44 = -0.48. So λ_F = -0.48/4.32 = -0.1111. Then F = P + λ_F u = (0,-0.566) + (-0.1111)*(1.2,1.697) = (-0.1333, -0.566 -0.1887) = (-0.1333, -0.7547). So F ≈ (-0.133, -0.755). B = (x_A, -y_A) = (1.2, -1.131). So we have B(1.2,-1.131), E(-0.811,-1.713), F(-0.133,-0.755). Now circle through these points. Let's compute its center and radius. Compute perpendicular bisectors. But we can also compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF). H = (0, -y_A/(2c)) = (0, -1.13137/(4)) = (0, -0.2828). So H=(0,-0.2828). (1) 4.022X + 1.164Y = -0.873
(2) 2.666X - 0.752Y = 2.1313

Solve. From (2): X = (2.1313+0.752Y)/2.666 = (2.1313/2.666) + (0.752/2.666)Y ≈ 0.799+0.282Y. That's very close to radius 1.109. The small difference is due to rounding. So indeed, L is tangent to circle (BEF). So the algebraic approach works.

Now we need to prove it generally. Given the parameterization with m and c, we have:

So M is not at origin; we have translation. To set M at origin, we would translate horizontally by -m. That would change the midpoint of CD. So we have two different coordinate systems. In the numeric example, we used the parameterization with m and c and then computed points; we didn't set M at origin. We used the coordinate system where midpoint of CD is origin. So we have:

- M = (m,0), N = (m+1,0), C = (-c,0), D = (c,0).

Now, the circle (BEF). Since B = (x_A, -y_A). And E and F lie on line through P with direction u. So we can try to find the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Perhaps we can find its equation in a nice form using complex numbers. Let's use complex numbers with the same coordinate system. Let’s denote:

- Let’s set the complex plane with origin at the midpoint of CD (which is the y-axis). Then:

- P = 0 + i p_y, with p_y = 2c m(m+1)/y_A = -2c α / y_A? Because m(m+1) = -α, so p_y = -2c α / y_A. Since y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α), we have p_y = -2c α / (2√(c(c-1)α)) = -c √α / √(c(c-1)) = -√(c) √α / √(c-1)? Actually p_y = -c α / √(c(c-1)α) = -c √α / √(c(c-1)). That's fine.

Now, we need to find the circle through B = x_A - i y_A, E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u. Since P = i p_y, and λ_E, λ_F are real.

We want to prove that the line through H parallel to u is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the line L: H + t u (t real) is tangent to that circle. Given the time, I think we can produce a proof using coordinate geometry with the parameterization we have. We have already simplified many expressions. Now we can compute the circle (BEF) explicitly. Since E and F are points on line through P with direction u, we can write them as:

E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u.

We can find λ_E and λ_F using the power of P with respect to Ω and Γ. But we can also compute them directly from the geometry using the fact that P is the circumcenter of ACD, so we have relations.

Let's compute λ_E and λ_F in terms of m and c. We have:

- P = i p_y, with p_y = -2c α / y_A, where α = -m(m+1) >0, y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α). So p_y = -2c α / (2√(c(c-1)α)) = -c√α / √(c(c-1)). So p_y = -√(c) √α / √(c-1). That's a negative real number times i? Actually p_y is a real number, so P = i * (negative). So P is on the negative imaginary axis.

- r = m+c. But m is negative, c>1, so r = c + m. Since m∈(-1,0), r∈(c-1, c). So r>0.

- We need |P|^2 - r^2 = p_y^2 - r^2. Compute p_y^2 = c^2 α / (c(c-1)) = c α/(c-1). So p_y^2 = c α/(c-1). And r^2 = (m+c)^2 = m^2 + 2mc + c^2. But m^2+m = -α? Actually α = -m(m+1) = -m^2 - m. So m^2 = -α - m. Then r^2 = -α - m + 2mc + c^2 = c^2 + 2mc - m - α. Not nice.

But maybe we can find a simpler expression for λ_E using the fact that E is on Ω. Since A is on Ω, and P is circumcenter, we have that PA = PC = PD. Also, we can compute the power of P with respect to Ω: Pow_Ω(P) = PM^2 - r^2. But we can also compute using the fact that the circle (ACD) is centered at P. Since C lies on Ω and also on the circle (ACD), we have that P is the circumcenter, so PC = PA. And M is the center of Ω, so M is on the perpendicular bisector of AC. That gives some relations.

Alternatively, we can compute λ_E using the coordinates we already have: We have u = (x_A, k y_A). And we have P = (0,p_y). So |u|^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 y_A^2. Now we need p_y^2 - r^2. But r = m+c. We can compute r^2 = (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2. Also, from the circle Ω, we have (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2. So r^2 = x_A^2 -2m x_A + m^2 + y_A^2. So r^2 = x_A^2 + y_A^2 -2m x_A + m^2. Thus p_y^2 - r^2 = p_y^2 - (x_A^2+y_A^2) + 2m x_A - m^2. But note that p_y^2 =? We have relation p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A+r). But p_x = 0 in this coordinate system? Wait, we have P = (0, p_y) because we set midpoint of CD as origin, and P lies on y-axis. So p_x=0. So the relation p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A+r) becomes p_y y_A = 0 => p_y=0 or y_A=0. But y_A>0, so p_y=0. That would imply P at origin, which is not true in our parameterization because P is at (0,p_y) with p_y≠0. So there is a mistake: In our coordinate system, we set the midpoint of CD as origin, and we have C=(-c,0), D=(c,0). Then P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is the y-axis, so P=(0,p_y). That's fine. But then we also have the relation from the fact that A lies on circle centered at P: |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2. This gave us p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A+r). But p_x=0, so that would give p_y y_A = 0 => p_y=0. So this suggests that in our coordinate system, P must be at origin? But earlier we derived that p_y = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. That is not zero generally. So there is an inconsistency: The relation p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A+r) came from setting |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2, with P=(p_x,p_y), C=(-c,0). Let's re-derive that relation carefully with our coordinate system.

We have C = (-c,0). P = (p_x,p_y). Condition |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2 gives: (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = (p_x + c)^2 + p_y^2. Expand: p_x^2 -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 + p_y^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = p_x^2 +2p_x c + c^2 + p_y^2 => -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = 2p_x c + c^2 => x_A^2+y_A^2 -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x c + c^2. But x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2 (since A on Ω). So r^2 -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x c + c^2 => -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x c + c^2 - r^2 => divide 2: -p_x x_A - p_y y_A = p_x c + (c^2 - r^2)/2. So p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A + c) - (c^2 - r^2)/2. So the relation is not simply p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A+r). There is an extra term involving (c^2 - r^2)/2. So our earlier simplification missed that. Let's re-derive correctly.

We have A on Ω: |A-M|^2 = r^2. And also A on circle centered at P: |A-P|^2 = |C-P|^2. So we have two equations. Let's do it properly with our parameterization where M=(m,0), N=(m+1,0), C=(-c,0), D=(c,0), and we have A=(x_A,y_A) with y_A>0. We derived x_A = c(2m+1) from the circle equations. And we have y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)(-m(m+1)). So that's fine.

Now P is circumcenter of ACD. Since C and D are symmetric about y-axis, P lies on y-axis: P=(0,p_y). Then condition |A-P|^2 = |C-P|^2 gives: x_A^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2 = c^2 + p_y^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 - 2y_A p_y = c^2 => 2y_A p_y = x_A^2 + y_A^2 - c^2. But x_A^2+y_A^2 =? Since A is on Ω: (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => x_A^2 -2m x_A + m^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2 + 2m x_A - m^2. So then p_y = (r^2 + 2m x_A - m^2 - c^2)/(2y_A). But we also have r = m+c, so r^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2. Then r^2 - m^2 = 2mc+c^2. So p_y = (2mc+c^2 + 2m x_A - c^2)/(2y_A) = (2m(x_A+c))/(2y_A) = m(x_A+c)/y_A. That matches our earlier expression. So p_y = m(x_A+c)/y_A. Now x_A+c = c(2m+1)+c = 2c(m+1). So p_y = m * 2c(m+1)/y_A = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. Since m(m+1) is negative, p_y negative. So that's consistent.

Thus the relation is p_y = m(x_A+c)/y_A. So p_y y_A = m(x_A+c). That's the relation we have.

Now we also have from the condition that A is on Ω: (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = (m+c)^2. So (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2 => x_A^2 -2m x_A + m^2 + y_A^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 -2m x_A = 2mc+c^2 => x_A^2+y_A^2 = 2m x_A + 2mc + c^2. So that's fine.

Now we need to compute λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. |P|^2 = p_y^2. r^2 = (m+c)^2. So |P|^2 - r^2 = p_y^2 - (m+c)^2. We can compute p_y^2 = (2c m(m+1)/y_A)^2 = 4c^2 m^2 (m+1)^2 / y_A^2. And (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2. So we need to simplify p_y^2 - (m+c)^2. We have y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)(-m(m+1)) = 4c(c-1)α, with α = -m(m+1). So y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α. Also, m(m+1) = -α. So p_y^2 = 4c^2 α^2 / (4c(c-1)α) = c α/(c-1). So p_y^2 = c α/(c-1). That's simpler. So |P|^2 - r^2 = c α/(c-1) - (m+c)^2. Now (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2. But we can also express m^2 in terms of α: m^2 = -α - m. So (m+c)^2 = -α - m + 2mc + c^2 = c^2 + 2mc - m - α. So |P|^2 - r^2 = c α/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m + α. So = c α/(c-1) + α - c^2 - 2mc + m. Not obviously factorable.

Now also note that we have relation from A on Ω: x_A^2+y_A^2 = 2m x_A + 2mc + c^2. But x_A = c(2m+1). So x_A^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2. And y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α. So x_A^2+y_A^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + 4c(c-1)α. Also, 2m x_A = 2m c(2m+1)=2c m(2m+1). So the equation becomes: c^2(2m+1)^2 + 4c(c-1)α = 2c m(2m+1) + 2mc + c^2. This can be used to relate α and m. Let's compute α = -m(m+1). So m(m+1) = -α. Then we can express everything in terms of m and c. It might be easier to work directly with m and c.

Given that we have explicit expressions for λ_E and λ_F, we can compute them in terms of m and c. Let's do that.

First, compute |u|^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 y_A^2, where k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). x_A = c(2m+1). y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)(-m(m+1)) = 4c(c-1)α, with α = -m(m+1). So:

|u|^2 = c^2 (2m+1)^2 + k^2 * 4c(c-1)α.

Now we need λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2. p_y^2 = c α/(c-1). So λ_E = (c α/(c-1) - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2.

Similarly, λ_F = (|P-N|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. Compute |P-N|^2 = (0 - (m+1))^2 + (p_y - 0)^2 = (m+1)^2 + p_y^2. R^2 = (c - (m+1))^2 = (c - m -1)^2. So |P-N|^2 - R^2 = (m+1)^2 + p_y^2 - (c-m-1)^2 = (m+1)^2 - (c-m-1)^2 + p_y^2. Compute (m+1)^2 - (c-m-1)^2 = [(m+1) - (c-m-1)] * [(m+1)+(c-m-1)] = (m+1 - c + m +1) * (c) = (2m+2 - c) * c = c(2m+2-c). So |P-N|^2 - R^2 = c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2. And p_y^2 = c α/(c-1). So λ_F = (c(2m+2-c) + c α/(c-1)) / |u|^2 = c(2m+2-c + α/(c-1))/|u|^2.

Now note that α = -m(m+1). So we can compute these.

But maybe we can find λ_E and λ_F in a simpler form by using the fact that E and F are the second intersections of line AP with circles. There is a known formula: If a line through A meets a circle again at E, and if P is the circumcenter of ACD, then perhaps the points are such that E, C, and something are collinear? Let's test with numeric: In our numeric example, A=(1.2,1.131), C=(-2,0), D=(2,0). P=(0,-0.566). Line AP: from A to P. E=(-0.811,-1.713). Is there a relation? Not obviously.

We have:

- α = -m(m+1) >0.
- y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α). Let’s denote y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α). So y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α.
- x_A = c(2m+1). Note that 2m+1 ∈ (-1,1). So x_A can be positive or negative.
- p_y = 2c m(m+1)/y_A = -2c α / y_A = -2c α / (2√(c(c-1)α)) = -c√α/√(c(c-1)). So p_y = -c√α/√(c(c-1)). So p_y^2 = c^2 α/(c(c-1)) = cα/(c-1). Good.
- k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). So u = x_A + i k y_A. |u|^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 y_A^2.
- λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2. Compute (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2. And p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). So λ_E = (cα/(c-1) - (m^2+2mc+c^2))/|u|^2.
- λ_F = (c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2)/|u|^2 = (c(2m+2-c) + cα/(c-1))/|u|^2 = c(2m+2-c + α/(c-1))/|u|^2.

Now, we can also express m^2 in terms of α: m^2 = -α - m. So (m^2+2mc+c^2) = -α - m + 2mc + c^2 = c^2 + 2mc - m - α. So λ_E = (cα/(c-1) - (c^2+2mc-m-α))/|u|^2 = (cα/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m + α)/|u|^2 = (α (c/(c-1) + 1) - c^2 - 2mc + m)/|u|^2 = (α (c/(c-1) + (c-1)/(c-1))? And -c^2 -2mc + m = -c^2 -2mc + m. So λ_E = ( -m(m+1)(2c-1)/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m )/|u|^2. That is not obviously factorable.

Similarly, λ_F = c(2m+2-c + α/(c-1))/|u|^2 = c(2m+2-c + (-m(m+1))/(c-1))/|u|^2. So λ_F = c(2m+2-c - m(m+1)/(c-1))/|u|^2.

Given the time, I think we should produce a coordinate solution that is rigorous and complete. I'll do it with a systematic approach.

I'll set up coordinate system: Let MN be the x-axis, with M at (0,0), N at (d,0), where d>0. Since the radius of Ω is less than that of Γ, we have r<R. The circles intersect at two points, so d^2 < (R+r)^2 and d^2 > (R-r)^2. Also, the line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C-M-N-D. Since M is at (0,0), C is on the ray from M opposite N, so C = (-r, 0). Since N is at (d,0), D is on the ray from N away from M, so D = (d+R, 0). So we have:

C = (-r, 0), M = (0,0), N = (d,0), D = (d+R, 0).

Now, let A be the upper intersection of the circles. Then A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A>0, and:

x_A^2 + y_A^2 = r^2, (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2.

Subtracting: (x_A - d)^2 - x_A^2 = R^2 - r^2 => -2d x_A + d^2 = R^2 - r^2 => x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). Then y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). So we have explicit.

Now, P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line at x = (C+D)/2 = (-r + d+R)/2 = (d+R-r)/2. So p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Then p_y is determined by PA = PC. PC^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + p_y^2. PA^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2. Set equal:

(p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + p_y^2.

We can solve for p_y. Expanding: (p_x - x_A)^2 + p_y^2 - 2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + p_y^2 => cancel p_y^2: (p_x - x_A)^2 - 2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2. So 2p_y y_A = (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)/2)^2. Then p_y = [ (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)/2)^2 ] / (2 y_A). We can compute this explicitly. But we already derived a nicer expression: p_y = - (x_A + r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A). Let's verify: Since p_x = (d+R-r)/2, we have p_x - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - x_A. And we had earlier p_y = - (x_A + r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A). This should be correct if we use the relation from the circle equations. Let's derive quickly: Using the fact that A lies on Ω, we have x_A^2+y_A^2=r^2. Also, from the condition that P is circumcenter, we have |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2. This gave: (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 => p_x^2 -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 + p_y^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = p_x^2 +2p_x r + r^2 + p_y^2 => -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = 2p_x r + r^2 => x_A^2+y_A^2 -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x r + r^2 => r^2 -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x r + r^2 => -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x r => divide 2: -p_x x_A - p_y y_A = p_x r => p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A + r). So p_y = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A. Since p_x = (d+R-r)/2, we get p_y = - (x_A+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A). Yes. So that's correct.

Thus P = ( (d+R-r)/2, - (x_A+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A) ).

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=(0,0), N=(d,0) are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical line x = p_x, so H lies on x = p_x. Let H = (p_x, h_y). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (d - p_x, 0 - p_y) = (d - p_x, -p_y). Since M=(0,0), the line MH is perpendicular to PN, so (p_x, h_y) dot (d - p_x, -p_y) = 0 => p_x (d - p_x) - h_y p_y = 0 => h_y = p_x (d - p_x)/p_y. Since p_y negative, h_y is negative if p_x(d-p_x)>0. So H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y). That matches our earlier.

Now, line AP: direction u = A - P = (x_A - p_x, y_A - p_y). Since P is on vertical line, we can compute.

Points E and F: second intersections of line AP with circles Ω and Γ. We can compute using power. For Ω: Let λ be such that point X = P + λ u lies on Ω. Then |X - M|^2 = r^2. Since A = P + u, we have λ=1 gives A. So the quadratic in λ: |P - M + λ u|^2 = r^2 => |P-M|^2 + 2λ Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + λ^2 |u|^2 = r^2. But A satisfies: |P-M+u|^2 = r^2 => |P-M|^2 + 2 Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + |u|^2 = r^2. So the quadratic can be written as: λ^2 |u|^2 + 2λ Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + (|P-M|^2 - r^2) = 0. The constant term is |P-M|^2 - r^2 = -Pow_Ω(P). The product of roots = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2 = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. Since one root is λ=1, the other root λ_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. Similarly, for Γ: λ_F = -Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2. So E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u.

Now, we need to prove that the line through H parallel to u is tangent to circle (BEF). Since we have all points in terms of d,r,R (or equivalently d, r, R), we can attempt to compute the condition.

I think the key is to use the fact that the points B, E, F are concyclic and that the line through H parallel to AP is the polar of H with respect to the circle (BEF). But we need a simpler relation.

Observe that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN. There is a known relation: The circumcircle of triangle PMN is the circle with diameter? Not.

Another known fact: The reflection of H across MN is on the circumcircle of PMN. That point is H' = (p_x, -h_y). Also, note that H' lies on the circumcircle of PMN. Now, consider the line AP. Since P is the circumcenter of ACD, and M,N are centers, maybe the line AP is the line through P and the midpoint of something? 

Maybe we can show that the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of H' with respect to triangle PMN. Let's test with numeric: In our numeric example, triangle PMN: P=(0,-0.566), M=(-0.2,0), N=(0.8,0). The circumcircle of PMN: we can compute its equation. H' = reflection of H across MN: H=(0,-0.2828) so H' = (0,0.2828). Does H' lie on circumcircle? Let's check distances: For triangle PMN, the circumcenter is somewhere. In our numeric, we computed O_PMN =? Actually we can compute the circumcircle of PMN. But the Simson line of H' with respect to triangle PMN: the feet of perpendiculars from H' to the sides. The side MN is x-axis, foot is (0,0) (since H' has x=0, y=0.2828, projection onto x-axis is (0,0)). The side PN: line through P and N. Compute PN: P(0,-0.566), N(0.8,0). Its equation: y = (0.566/0.8)x -0.566? Actually slope = (0 - (-0.566))/(0.8-0)=0.566/0.8=0.7075. So line: y = 0.7075x -0.566. The foot from H' (0,0.2828) to this line: we can compute. The foot from H' to MN is (0,0). So the Simson line is the line through these feet. The line through (0,0) and the foot to PN? That would be some line. Is it parallel to AP? AP direction u=(1.2,1.697) slope=1.414. The line through (0,0) and the foot to PN: let's compute foot to PN. The line PN: y = 0.7075x -0.566. Write in standard form: 0.7075x - y -0.566=0. Foot from (0,0.2828): the perpendicular line has direction (0.7075, -1) because normal is (0.7075, -1). Param: (0,0.2828)+ t(0.7075, -1). Intersection with PN: 0.7075(0.7075t) - (0.2828 - t) -0.566=0 => 0.5t -0.2828 + t -0.566=0 => 1.5t -0.8488=0 => t=0.5659. Then foot = (0.5659*0.7075=0.4, 0.2828 -0.5659=-0.2831). So foot ≈ (0.4, -0.283). Then line through (0,0) and (0.4,-0.283) has slope -0.283/0.4=-0.7075. That is not parallel to AP (slope 1.414). So not.

Thus that's not.

Let's try to search for known results: In a configuration of two intersecting circles, the line through the orthocenter of the triangle formed by the centers and the circumcenter of a certain triangle parallel to the line joining an intersection point and that circumcenter is tangent to the circumcircle of the other intersection and the two points where that line meets the circles again. This might be a known problem from IMO Shortlist 2010 or something. I recall a problem: "Let Ω and Γ be two circles intersecting at A and B. The line through the centers meets Ω at C and Γ at D. Let P be the circumcenter of triangle ACD. The line AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. Let H be the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." This is likely an IMO Shortlist problem. The solution probably uses spiral similarity and properties of orthocenter. Let's try to derive synthetic proof.

Let’s denote the circumcircle of triangle ACD as ω. Its center is P. Since A, C, D are on ω, we have PA = PC = PD.

Now, consider the line AP. Since E and F are on AP, we have that A, E, F are collinear. Also, note that B is the other intersection of Ω and Γ. The points E and F are the second intersections with Ω and Γ. Then the circle through B, E, F is known to be the circle that is the image of the circle (ACD) under some transformation? Let's examine the spiral similarity that sends Ω to Γ. The center of this spiral similarity is A (or B). Under the spiral similarity with center A that sends Ω to Γ, the point C (on Ω) maps to some point on Γ, say C'. Is that point D? Not generally. But we can consider the spiral similarity with center B that sends Ω to Γ. Since B is also an intersection, the spiral similarity centered at B with factor R/r sends M to N. Under this, C (on Ω) maps to some point on Γ. That point might be D? Let's check: In numeric, B=(1.2,-1.131), C=(-2,0). The line BC: from B to C. Does it intersect Γ at D? D=(2,0). The line BC: param B + t(C-B) = (1.2-3.2t, -1.131+1.131t). For t=0.5, we get (-1, -0.5655). Not (2,0). So no.

Thus not.

Another idea: Use the fact that P is the circumcenter of ACD, so the circle ω is the circle with diameter? Not.

Consider the reflection of P across the midpoint of MN? Since H is orthocenter, the point P' = reflection of P across the midpoint of MN might be something like the point where the altitudes meet? Let’s compute the circumcircle of PMN. Let its center be O_PMN. Then O_PMN is the circumcenter. The orthocenter H of PMN satisfies: H = P + M + N - 2 O_PMN? Actually, in any triangle, the vector from circumcenter to orthocenter is the sum of the vectors from circumcenter to the vertices. So O_PMN H = O_PMN P + O_PMN M + O_PMN N. So H = O_PMN + (P - O_PMN) + (M - O_PMN) + (N - O_PMN) = P + M + N - 2 O_PMN. So O_PMN = (P+M+N - H)/2. Not directly.

Given the time, I think I will produce a solution that uses coordinates but with a clever normalization that reduces the algebra. I'll use the coordinate system where the midpoint of CD is origin and x-axis is MN. Then we have parameters m (negative) and c>1. We derived expressions for points. Now we can compute the circle (BEF) by using the fact that the circumcircle of three points can be found by solving for the circle equation. Since we have symbolic expressions, we can attempt to compute the center O. Let's try to compute O in terms of m and c.

We have:

B = (x_A, -y_A) = (c(2m+1), -y_A)
E = (λ_E x_A, p_y + λ_E k y_A)
F = (λ_F x_A, p_y + λ_F k y_A)

We can find the circumcenter O = (X,Y) by solving the linear equations from the equalities of distances. Since B and E and F are not symmetric, but we can compute using the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates. Alternatively, we can use the fact that the circle through B and two points on line AP can be described by the equation that the power of any point on AP is constant. Since E and F are on AP, the line AP is the chord of the circle (BEF) that passes through B? Actually, B is not on AP generally. So the circle (BEF) has chord EF on line AP. The perpendicular bisector of EF is a line perpendicular to AP at the midpoint of EF. That line is not necessarily vertical. The circumcenter O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BF (or BE). So we can find O by solving two linear equations.

Let’s compute the coordinates explicitly using the expressions we have. We have:

x_A = c(2m+1)
y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α) with α = -m(m+1). But we can also keep y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α.

We also have:

p_y = -2cα/y_A = -c√α/√(c(c-1)). That's negative.

k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1))

Now compute λ_E and λ_F. We have:

λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2
λ_F = (c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2)/|u|^2

But we also have |u|^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 y_A^2. And p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). And (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2.

We can try to simplify λ_E and λ_F using the relations between m and α. Since α = -m(m+1), we have m^2 = -α - m. So (m+c)^2 = -α - m + 2mc + c^2 = c^2 + 2mc - m - α.

Thus p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 = cα/(c-1) - (c^2+2mc - m - α) = cα/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m + α.

Similarly, for λ_F: c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2 = 2cm + 2c - c^2 + cα/(c-1).

Now, we also have expressions for x_A and y_A in terms of m,c. x_A = c(2m+1). So x_A^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2. y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α.

Thus |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + k^2 * 4c(c-1)α. And k^2 = (2c-1)^2/(4(c-1)^2). So k^2 * 4c(c-1)α = (2c-1)^2/(4(c-1)^2) * 4c(c-1)α = (2c-1)^2 * c α/(c-1). So |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + c α (2c-1)^2/(c-1). That is |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + c α (2c-1)^2/(c-1).

Now, note that α = -m(m+1). So we can write everything in terms of m and c. This is messy but maybe we can find λ_E and λ_F in a simpler form by using the fact that u = x_A + i k y_A, and we have p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A+r) but p_x=0? No, we are in the coordinate system where midpoint of CD is origin, so p_x=0, and the relation p_y y_A = m(x_A+c) is the one we have. But we also have the circle Ω equation: (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = (m+c)^2. This gives a relation between x_A, y_A, m, c. Using x_A = c(2m+1), we can derive that y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α. So that's consistent.

Now, perhaps we can compute the coordinates of E and F more directly by using the fact that they are the second intersections of line AP with circles. Since we have A and P, we can also find the equation of line AP and then find the intersections with circles. That might be simpler: we can find the parametric line AP: P + t u. Then we can find t such that point lies on Ω: |P + t u - M|^2 = r^2. Since M=(m,0), and P=(0,p_y). So P - M = (-m, p_y). So we have: (-m + t x_A)^2 + (p_y + t k y_A)^2 = r^2. We know that t=1 gives A, so that satisfies. So we can solve for the other root t_E. This is a quadratic in t. The product of roots = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2 = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. So t_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. Similarly, for Γ: M' = N? Actually N=(m+1,0), so P - N = (-(m+1), p_y). Then condition: (-(m+1) + t x_A)^2 + (p_y + t k y_A)^2 = R^2. The product of roots = (|P-N|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2 = -Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2, so t_F = -Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2. So we have t_E and t_F as above.

Now, we can compute Pow_Ω(P) = |P-M|^2 - r^2 = (m^2 + p_y^2) - (m+c)^2 = m^2 + p_y^2 - m^2 - 2mc - c^2 = p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2. And Pow_Γ(P) = |P-N|^2 - R^2 = ((m+1)^2 + p_y^2) - (c-(m+1))^2 = (m+1)^2 + p_y^2 - (c^2 - 2c(m+1) + (m+1)^2) = p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1). So t_E = - (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2, t_F = - (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2.

But earlier we had λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2? That gave λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m^2+2mc+c^2))/|u|^2 = (p_y^2 - m^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. But p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2 =? Note that (m^2+2mc+c^2) = (m+c)^2, so p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 = p_y^2 - m^2 -2mc - c^2. So our t_E = - (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2 = - (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 + m^2)/|u|^2? Actually p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2 = p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 + m^2. So t_E = - (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 + m^2)/|u|^2 = - (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2 - m^2/|u|^2. That is different from λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2. So which is correct? Let's re-derive the quadratic for intersection with Ω.

We have line AP: X = P + t u, where u = A-P. We want X such that |X-M|^2 = r^2. Write X-M = (P-M) + t u. Then |P-M + t u|^2 = |P-M|^2 + 2t Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + t^2 |u|^2 = r^2. So the quadratic is: |u|^2 t^2 + 2 Re((P-M) \bar{u}) t + (|P-M|^2 - r^2) = 0. One root is t=1 (since A is on Ω). So the product of roots = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. Let the other root be t_E. Then 1 * t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. So t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. But careful: The product of roots is constant term/leading coefficient = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. So t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. That's the correct relation. Earlier I used -Pow_Ω(P) which is -( |P-M|^2 - r^2 ). So t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. That is positive? Let's check numeric: |P-M|^2 = p_y^2 = 0.320, r^2=3.24, so |P-M|^2 - r^2 = -2.92, t_E = -2.92/4.32 = -0.6759. That's negative, so t_E negative. That's fine. So t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. And we have λ_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2? Since Pow_Ω(P) = |P-M|^2 - r^2, so λ_E = - (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2 = -t_E. So there is a sign difference. In our earlier parameterization, we used E = P + λ_E u, and we defined λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2? That was from a different approach. Let's stick with the quadratic: t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. So E = P + t_E u. Similarly, t_F = (|P-N|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. So we can use these.

Now, with our coordinate system where M=(m,0), N=(m+1,0), P=(0,p_y). Then:

|P-M|^2 = m^2 + p_y^2, so t_E = (m^2 + p_y^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. But r^2 = (m+c)^2, so t_E = (m^2 + p_y^2 - (m^2+2mc+c^2))/|u|^2 = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. That's what we had earlier as λ_E? Actually we had λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2? That gave p_y^2 - m^2 -2mc -c^2. So different. So t_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. Since p_y^2 = cα/(c-1) and 2mc + c^2 = c(2m+c). So t_E = (cα/(c-1) - c(2m+c))/|u|^2 = c(α/(c-1) - (2m+c))/|u|^2. And α = -m(m+1), so α/(c-1) - (2m+c) = -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2m - c = -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2m - c. That is negative. So t_E negative.

Now, compute t_F = (|P-N|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2 = ((m+1)^2 + p_y^2 - (c-(m+1))^2)/|u|^2 = (m+1)^2 + p_y^2 - (c^2 -2c(m+1)+(m+1)^2)/|u|^2 = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2. So t_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2.

Now, note that p_y = m(x_A+c)/y_A = m*2c(m+1)/y_A = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. So p_y^2 = 4c^2 m^2 (m+1)^2 / y_A^2. But we already have p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). That's simpler.

Thus t_E = (cα/(c-1) - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. And t_F = (cα/(c-1) - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2.

Now, we also have |u|^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 y_A^2. With x_A = c(2m+1), y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α, and k^2 = (2c-1)^2/(4(c-1)^2). So |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + cα (2c-1)^2/(c-1). 

Now, B = (x_A, -y_A) = (c(2m+1), -y_A). So we have all points.

Now, the circle (BEF). We want to show that the line through H parallel to u is tangent. Since H = (p_x, h_y) with p_x = (d+R-r)/2? Wait, in this coordinate system, we set the midpoint of CD as origin, so p_x=0. So P=(0,p_y). Then H = (0, h_y) with h_y = p_x(d-p_x)/p_y? But p_x=0, so h_y = 0? Actually from earlier, H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y) = (0,0). That would imply H is at origin. But is that true? In our numeric example, with m=-0.2, c=2, we had P=(0,-0.566), H=(0,-0.2828). So H is not at origin. So there is a mistake: In the coordinate system where the midpoint of CD is origin, we have C=(-c,0), D=(c,0). But we also have M=(m,0) with m not necessarily 0? In our numeric, m=-0.2, so M is not at origin. Then P is on y-axis, so p_x=0. Then the altitude from P to MN is vertical line x=0, so H has x-coordinate 0. But H's y-coordinate is h_y = p_x (d-p_x)/p_y? Wait, we derived H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y) when M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(p_x,p_y). That was with M at origin. In our current coordinate system, M is not at origin; it's at (m,0). So we need to recompute H in this coordinate system. Since M and N are on x-axis at (m,0) and (m+1,0), and P=(0,p_y). Then the altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (m+1, -p_y). So condition: (H - M) dot PN = 0. Let H=(0,h_y). Then H-M = (-m, h_y). Dot with PN: (-m)*(m+1) + h_y*(-p_y) = 0 => -m(m+1) - h_y p_y = 0 => h_y = -m(m+1)/p_y = α/p_y? Since α = -m(m+1), so h_y = α/p_y. But p_y = 2c m(m+1)/y_A = -2c α / y_A. So h_y = α / (-2c α / y_A) = - y_A/(2c). So H = (0, - y_A/(2c)). That matches our earlier H. So H is not at origin. Good.

Thus we have:

- H = (0, - y_A/(2c)).
- u = (x_A, k y_A) with x_A = c(2m+1), k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)).
- E = P + t_E u = (t_E x_A, p_y + t_E k y_A).
- F = P + t_F u = (t_F x_A, p_y + t_F k y_A).
- B = (x_A, -y_A).

Now, we need to prove that line L: H + t u is tangent to circle through B,E,F.

Given the complexity, but we have simplified expressions: t_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2, t_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2. And p_y = m(x_A+c)/y_A = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. Also, note that α = -m(m+1), so p_y = -2cα/y_A.

Now, perhaps we can find that the points B,E,F have a property that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter? Let's compute the coordinates in terms of m and c. We can try to compute the circumcenter O of BEF. Let’s denote:

Let’s compute the coordinates explicitly by substituting p_y, etc. We have:

x_A = c(2m+1)
y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α) where α = -m(m+1). Let’s denote Y = y_A for brevity.

Then:
P = (0, p_y) with p_y = -2cα/Y = -c√α/√(c(c-1)). That's messy but we can keep as p_y = -2cα/Y.

Now, compute t_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. We have p_y^2 = 4c^2α^2/Y^2. And 2mc + c^2 = c(2m+c). So t_E = (4c^2α^2/Y^2 - c(2m+c))/|u|^2 = c(4cα^2/Y^2 - (2m+c))/|u|^2. But α = -m(m+1). So α^2 = m^2(m+1)^2. So 4cα^2/Y^2 = 4c m^2(m+1)^2 / (4c(c-1)α) = m^2(m+1)^2/( (c-1)α) )? Since Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, so α^2/Y^2 = α^2/(4c(c-1)α) = α/(4c(c-1)). Then 4cα^2/Y^2 = 4c * α/(4c(c-1)) = α/(c-1). So p_y^2 = α/(c-1). That's consistent. So p_y^2 = α/(c-1). Then t_E = (α/(c-1) - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. And α = -m(m+1). So α/(c-1) - 2mc - c^2 = -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2mc - c^2.

Similarly, t_F = (α/(c-1) - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2 = ( -m(m+1)/(c-1) - c^2 + 2c(m+1) )/|u|^2.

Let’s use the following: Since the points are not symmetric, but we can find the circumcenter O by using the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates: O = (X, Y_O) where X = ( (|B|^2 (E-F) + |E|^2 (F-B) + |F|^2 (B-E) ) / (2 (B × E + E × F + F × B) )? Actually, there is a formula using the fact that the circumcenter is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors. We can compute using the fact that the circumcenter is the point that is equidistant to B,E,F. So we have:

|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 => (X-x_A)^2 + (Y_O+Y)^2 = (X - t_E x_A)^2 + (Y_O - (p_y + t_E k Y))^2.

Expanding: X^2 -2X x_A + x_A^2 + Y_O^2 + 2Y_O Y + Y^2 = X^2 -2X t_E x_A + t_E^2 x_A^2 + Y_O^2 -2Y_O(p_y + t_E k Y) + (p_y + t_E k Y)^2.

Cancel X^2, Y_O^2: -2X x_A + x_A^2 + 2Y_O Y + Y^2 = -2X t_E x_A + t_E^2 x_A^2 -2Y_O(p_y + t_E k Y) + (p_y + t_E k Y)^2.

Bring terms: -2X x_A + 2X t_E x_A + 2Y_O Y + 2Y_O(p_y + t_E k Y) + x_A^2 + Y^2 - t_E^2 x_A^2 - (p_y + t_E k Y)^2 = 0 => 2X x_A (t_E -1) + 2Y_O (Y + p_y + t_E k Y) + (x_A^2(1-t_E^2) + Y^2 - (p_y + t_E k Y)^2) = 0.

Thus we have linear equation: 2x_A (t_E-1) X + 2(Y + p_y + t_E k Y) Y_O + C_E = 0, where C_E = x_A^2(1-t_E^2) + Y^2 - (p_y + t_E k Y)^2.

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2: 2x_A (t_F-1) X + 2(Y + p_y + t_F k Y) Y_O + C_F = 0, with C_F = x_A^2(1-t_F^2) + Y^2 - (p_y + t_F k Y)^2.

So we can solve for X and Y_O. This is doable if we compute these coefficients.

(2) (t_E x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_E k Y)^2 + A (t_E x_A) + B (p_y + t_E k Y) + C = 0.
(3) (t_F x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_F k Y)^2 + A (t_F x_A) + B (p_y + t_F k Y) + C = 0.

Subtract (1) from (2) and (3) to eliminate C. This gives two linear equations in A and B. Then we can solve for A and B. Then C can be found from (1). Then we can compute the quadratic in t when substituting L(t) = (t x_A, h_y + t k Y). That is:

Consider the following: Let’s denote the circle with diameter MN as ω1. Since H is orthocenter of triangle PMN, we have that the reflection of H across MN lies on the circumcircle of PMN. That reflection is H' = (p_x, -h_y). Also, note that the circumcircle of PMN is the circle with diameter? Not.

Now, consider the spiral similarity centered at A that sends M to N. This spiral similarity also sends the circle Ω to Γ. Under this, the point C (on Ω) maps to some point C' on Γ. Since C, M, N, D are collinear, and the spiral similarity sends M to N, the image of line MN is a line through N parallel to MN. That line is horizontal. So C' is the intersection of the horizontal line through N with Γ. That point is D? Not generally. Actually, the horizontal line through N is y = 0 (since N is on x-axis). So the horizontal line through N (x-axis) meets Γ at D and another point (n-R,0). Let's compute: The spiral similarity sends M to N, and also sends the circle Ω to Γ. So it sends the point C (on Ω) to some point on Γ. Since C is on Ω and also on line MN, its image will be on Γ and on the image of line MN, which is the line through N parallel to MN, i.e., horizontal line through N. So the image of C is the point where the horizontal line through N meets Γ, which is either D or the other point. Since C is on the left side of M, its image might be on the right side. In numeric, C=(-2,0), N=(0.8,0), the horizontal line through N is x-axis, and the intersection with Γ (center (0.8,0), radius 1.2) are at (0.8-1.2=-0.4,0) and (0.8+1.2=2.0,0). So the image of C under this spiral similarity is either (-0.4,0) or (2.0,0). Which one? The spiral similarity is centered at A=(1.2,1.131). It sends M=(-0.2,0) to N=(0.8,0). Let's compute the rotation and scaling. The vector from A to M: M-A = (-0.2-1.2, 0-1.131)=(-1.4,-1.131). From A to N: N-A=(0.8-1.2,0-1.131)=(-0.4,-1.131). So the spiral similarity sends M to N, and also sends any point X to Y such that ∠XAY = θ and AY/AX = k. Now, C=(-2,0). Compute vector AC = C-A = (-2-1.2, 0-1.131)=(-3.2,-1.131). Then its image under spiral similarity is point C' such that AC' = k * (AC rotated by θ). The rotated vector: rotate (-3.2,-1.131) by 31.6° counterclockwise. Compute: rotation matrix: (cosθ, -sinθ; sinθ, cosθ). cosθ=0.852, sinθ=0.524. So rotated = (0.852*(-3.2) - 0.524*(-1.131), 0.524*(-3.2)+0.852*(-1.131)) = (-2.726 +0.592, -1.677 -0.964)=(-2.134, -2.641). Then multiply by k=0.6667 gives (-1.422, -1.761). So C' = A + (-1.422,-1.761) = (1.2-1.422,1.131-1.761)=(-0.222,-0.630). That is not on x-axis. So the image of C is not on the x-axis because the line through A and C is not through A? Actually, the image of any point under a spiral similarity centered at A is on the ray from A through the point rotated by θ. So C' is on the ray from A through C rotated by θ. That ray is not horizontal. So the image of C is not on the horizontal line through N. That's because the spiral similarity sends M to N, but it does not send the line MN to itself; it sends the line MN to a line through N parallel to MN. So the image of C, which is on MN, must be on the image of line MN, which is the line through N parallel to MN. Since MN is horizontal, the image is horizontal line through N. But we computed C' and got a point not on that horizontal line. So something is wrong: The spiral similarity centered at A that sends M to N sends the line through M and N (which is MN) to a line through N that is the image of MN under the transformation. Since the transformation is a spiral similarity (rotation+scaling) about A, it sends any line not through A to a line parallel to it? Actually, a spiral similarity is a composition of rotation and scaling about A. It sends any line L to a line L' that is the image of L under the rotation+scaling. If L does not pass through A, then L' is not necessarily parallel to L; it is rotated by the same rotation angle. For a line L that does not pass through A, its image L' is a line that is rotated by the angle θ relative to L, but not necessarily parallel. So the image of MN under the spiral similarity is a line through N that is rotated by θ relative to MN. Since MN is horizontal, the image is a line through N at angle θ. So the image of C is on that line. So my earlier statement that the image of line MN is a line through N parallel to MN is false for a spiral similarity; it's true for a homothety (scaling) about A, but not for a rotation+scaling unless the rotation is 0. So the spiral similarity is not a homothety; it rotates as well. So the image of MN is a line through N that makes angle θ with MN. So the image of C lies on that line. So not on x-axis.

Thus that approach is not.

So Y(1-k) = -2cα/Y => Y^2(1-k) = -2cα. But Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, so 4c(c-1)α(1-k) = -2cα => divide cα (nonzero): 4(c-1)(1-k) = -2 => 2(c-1)(1-k) = -1 => (c-1)(1-k) = -1/2. Now compute k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). Then 1-k = 1 - (2c-1)/(2(c-1)) = (2(c-1) - (2c-1))/(2(c-1)) = (2c-2-2c+1)/(2(c-1)) = (-1)/(2(c-1)). So (c-1)(1-k) = (c-1)*(-1)/(2(c-1)) = -1/2. Yes, that holds. So consistent.

Now, u_y + p_y + Y = u_y + Y + p_y = kY + Y + p_y = Y(1+k) + p_y. But p_y = Y(1-k), so = Y(1+k+1-k) = 2Y. So u_y + p_y + Y = 2Y! That's a nice simplification. Indeed, u_y + p_y + Y = kY + Y + (Y - kY) = 2Y. So the denominator simplifies to 2Y.

Thus we have:

A = - x_A u_y / (u_y + p_y + Y) = - x_A u_y / (2Y) = - x_A (u_y)/(2Y).
But u_y = kY, so A = - x_A kY/(2Y) = - (x_A k)/2.

Similarly, B = x_A^2 / (u_y + p_y + Y) = x_A^2 / (2Y) = x_A^2/(2Y).

So we have simple expressions:

A = - (x_A k)/2
B = x_A^2/(2Y)

Now, we also need C. From (1): C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - A x_A + B Y. Plug A and B:

C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - (- (x_A k)/2) x_A + (x_A^2/(2Y)) Y = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 k)/2 + (x_A^2)/2 = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2.

So C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2.

Now, we have the circle equation:

X^2 + Y^2 + A X + B Y + C = 0.

Now, the line L: (x,y) = (t x_A, h_y + t u_y) = (t x_A, -Y/(2c) + t k Y). So param t.

Now, β = 2h_y u_y + A x_A + B u_y. Compute:

2h_y u_y = 2 * (-Y/(2c)) * (kY) = - (kY^2)/c.
A x_A = (- (x_A k)/2) * x_A = - (x_A^2 k)/2.
B u_y = (x_A^2/(2Y)) * (kY) = (x_A^2 k)/2.

So A x_A + B u_y = - (x_A^2 k)/2 + (x_A^2 k)/2 = 0. So β = 2h_y u_y = - (kY^2)/c.

Thus β = - (k Y^2)/c.

B h_y = (x_A^2/(2Y)) * (-Y/(2c)) = - (x_A^2)/(4c).
C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2.

So γ = Y^2/(4c^2) - x_A^2/(4c) - x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2 = combine terms: Y^2 terms: Y^2/(4c^2) - Y^2 = Y^2(1/(4c^2) - 1) = Y^2( (1 - 4c^2)/(4c^2) ). x_A^2 terms: - x_A^2/(4c) - x_A^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2 = x_A^2( -1/(4c) - 1 + (k+1)/2 ) = x_A^2( -1/(4c) - 1 + k/2 + 1/2 ) = x_A^2( k/2 - 1/(4c) - 1/2 ). So γ = Y^2(1-4c^2)/(4c^2) + x_A^2( k/2 - 1/(4c) - 1/2 ).

Now, we need to show that D = β^2 - 4αγ = 0.

Actually, for a line through H that is tangent to a circle, the point of tangency T is the point on the line where the line touches the circle. Then the segment HT is the tangent segment. The distance from O to the line is the perpendicular distance from O to the line, which is the distance from O to T (since T is the foot of perpendicular from O to the line because the radius to the point of tangency is perpendicular to the tangent). Let’s set:

Alternatively, we can use vector geometry: Let’s set up coordinate system with M at origin, MN along x-axis, so M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Then C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). A=(x_A, y_A). Then we have P=(p_x, p_y) with p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Now compute p_y.

First compute x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So p_x - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = [d(d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2)]/(2d) = [d^2 + dR - dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2]/(2d) = [dR - dr - r^2 + R^2]/(2d) = [R(d - r) - (r^2 - R^2)]/(2d) = [R(d - r) + (R^2 - r^2)]/(2d) = [R(d - r) + (R - r)(R + r)]/(2d) = (R - r)[ (d - r) + (R + r) ]/(2d) = (R - r)(d - r + R + r)/(2d) = (R - r)(d + R)/(2d). So p_x - x_A = (R - r)(d + R)/(2d). Good.

Also y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2. But maybe we can compute (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2. Note that (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 + (r^2 - x_A^2). We can also express r^2 - x_A^2 = (r - x_A)(r + x_A). Might simplify.

Let's compute: (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 + r^2 - x_A^2. We know p_x - x_A = (R - r)(d+R)/(2d). So square: ((R - r)^2 (d+R)^2)/(4d^2). So sum = ((R - r)^2 (d+R)^2)/(4d^2) + r^2 - x_A^2.

Now we need ((d+R+r)^2)/4. Let's compute that: ((d+R+r)^2)/4 = (d^2 + R^2 + r^2 + 2dR + 2dr + 2Rr)/4.

Then p_y = [ (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)^2)/4 ] / (2 y_A). Let's compute numerator N = (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)^2)/4.

But y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2, so N = (p_x - x_A)^2 + r^2 - x_A^2 - ((d+R+r)^2)/4.

We can also compute x_A^2. x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So x_A^2 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4d^2). That might be messy. Maybe there is a simpler geometric interpretation.

Alternatively, we can compute P as circumcenter of ACD directly using formulas. Since C and D are on x-axis, the circumcenter lies on perpendicular bisector of CD which is vertical line x = (x_C+x_D)/2. The y-coordinate can be found by using that PA = PC. So (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2. That gives a linear equation in p_y? Expand: (p_x - x_A)^2 + p_y^2 - 2 p_y y_A + y_A^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 => cancel p_y^2: (p_x - x_A)^2 - 2 p_y y_A + y_A^2 = (p_x + r)^2 => -2 p_y y_A = (p_x + r)^2 - (p_x - x_A)^2 - y_A^2 => p_y = [ (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (p_x + r)^2 ] / (2 y_A). That's the same.

Now p_x + r = (d+R+r)/2, p_x - x_A = (R - r)(d+R)/(2d). So we have:

p_y = [ ((R - r)^2 (d+R)^2/(4d^2) + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)^2)/4 ] / (2 y_A).

Now we can attempt to simplify y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)^2)/4. Since y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2. x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So we can compute y_A^2 = r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4d^2). This is reminiscent of formula for distance from intersection to line of centers. But maybe we can express everything in terms of d, r, R.

Let’s compute y_A^2 explicitly:

y_A^2 = r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4d^2) = [4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2]/(4d^2).

Now the numerator of p_y: N = (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (p_x+r)^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 - (p_x+r)^2 + y_A^2.

But (p_x - x_A)^2 - (p_x+r)^2 = [(p_x - x_A) - (p_x+r)] * [(p_x - x_A) + (p_x+r)] = (-x_A - r) * (2p_x - x_A + r). Since p_x = (d+R-r)/2, then 2p_x = d+R-r. So (2p_x - x_A + r) = (d+R-r - x_A + r) = d+R - x_A. And (-x_A - r) = -(x_A + r). So product = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A). So N = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + y_A^2.

Thus p_y = [ -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + y_A^2 ] / (2 y_A).

Now y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2. So numerator: -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + r^2 - x_A^2 = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + r^2 - x_A^2.

Expand: -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) = -(x_A(d+R - x_A) + r(d+R - x_A)) = -(x_A(d+R) - x_A^2 + r(d+R) - r x_A) = -x_A(d+R) + x_A^2 - r(d+R) + r x_A.

Add r^2 - x_A^2: gives -x_A(d+R) + x_A^2 - r(d+R) + r x_A + r^2 - x_A^2 = -x_A(d+R) - r(d+R) + r x_A + r^2 = -x_A(d+R) - r(d+R) + r x_A + r^2 = -(d+R)(x_A + r) + r x_A + r^2.

But we also have -r(d+R) + r x_A = -r(d+R) + r x_A = -r(d+R - x_A). Hmm, maybe keep as is.

Alternatively, we can factor: -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + r^2 - x_A^2 = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + (r - x_A)(r + x_A). Note that (r - x_A)(r + x_A) = -(x_A - r)(r + x_A). So we have: -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) - (x_A - r)(r + x_A) = -(x_A + r)[(d+R - x_A) + (x_A - r)] = -(x_A + r)(d+R - r). Because (d+R - x_A) + (x_A - r) = d+R - r. So numerator simplifies to -(x_A + r)(d+R - r). That is much simpler! Let's verify:

-(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + (r - x_A)(r + x_A) = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + (r^2 - x_A^2) = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + (r - x_A)(r + x_A). Write (r - x_A)(r + x_A) = -(x_A - r)(r + x_A) = -(x_A - r)(x_A + r). So sum = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) - (x_A - r)(x_A + r) = -(x_A + r)[(d+R - x_A) + (x_A - r)] = -(x_A + r)(d+R - r). Yes! Good.

Thus numerator = -(x_A + r)(d+R - r). So p_y = [-(x_A + r)(d+R - r)] / (2 y_A). So p_y = - (x_A + r)(d+R - r) / (2 y_A). Since y_A > 0, p_y is negative. So P is below the x-axis. That makes sense: A is above, C and D on x-axis, so circumcenter might be below? Possibly.

So P = ( (d+R-r)/2 , - (x_A + r)(d+R - r) / (2 y_A) ).

Now we have M = (0,0), N = (d,0). So triangle PMN: P, M, N. We need orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, etc. We can compute H coordinates.

Let’s denote coordinates: M(0,0), N(d,0), P(p_x, p_y) with p_y negative. Let’s denote p_x = (d+R-r)/2, p_y = -k, where k = (x_A + r)(d+R - r)/(2 y_A) > 0. So P = (p_x, -k).

Now we need H, orthocenter. For triangle with vertices at (0,0), (d,0), and (p_x, -k). Since two vertices lie on x-axis, the orthocenter can be found by intersecting altitudes. The altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. The altitude from N is line through N perpendicular to PM. The altitude from P is vertical? Actually since M and N are on x-axis, the side MN is horizontal, so the altitude from P to MN is vertical line through P: x = p_x. So H lies on the vertical line x = p_x (since altitude from P is perpendicular to MN, and MN is horizontal, so altitude from P is vertical). So H = (p_x, h_y) for some h_y. Also H lies on altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. So we can compute h_y by using that vector MH is perpendicular to PN.

Compute PN: from P to N: N - P = (d - p_x, 0 - (-k)) = (d - p_x, k). So PN vector = (d - p_x, k). Then altitude from M: direction perpendicular to PN. So vector MH = (p_x - 0, h_y - 0) = (p_x, h_y). Condition: (p_x, h_y) dot (d - p_x, k) = 0 => p_x (d - p_x) + h_y k = 0 => h_y = - p_x (d - p_x) / k.

Thus H = (p_x, - p_x (d - p_x)/k).

Now p_x = (d+R-r)/2. So d - p_x = d - (d+R-r)/2 = (2d - d - R + r)/2 = (d - R + r)/2. So p_x (d - p_x) = ((d+R-r)/2) * ((d - R + r)/2) = ((d+R-r)(d - R + r))/4 = ((d + (R-r)) (d - (R-r)))/4 = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)/4.

Thus h_y = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)/(4k). Since k = (x_A + r)(d+R - r)/(2 y_A). So h_y = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)/(4 * ((x_A + r)(d+R - r)/(2 y_A))) = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2) * (2 y_A) / (4 (x_A + r)(d+R - r)) = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2) y_A / (2 (x_A + r)(d+R - r)).

Thus H = (p_x, - (d^2 - (R-r)^2) y_A / (2 (x_A + r)(d+R - r)) ). Note that h_y is negative (since y_A>0, denominator positive). So H is also below x-axis, on same vertical line as P.

Now we need line through H parallel to AP. AP is line through A and P. Compute slope of AP. A = (x_A, y_A), P = (p_x, -k). So slope m_AP = ( -k - y_A ) / (p_x - x_A). But we might not need explicit; we can find line ℓ: through H with same slope as AP. Since H is on x = p_x, and ℓ is parallel to AP, its equation: y - h_y = m_AP (x - p_x). But since x = p_x gives y = h_y, that's consistent. So ℓ: y = h_y + m_AP (x - p_x). We need to show that this line is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. B = (x_A, -y_A). E is second intersection of line AP with Ω (circle centered at M(0,0) radius r). F is second intersection of line AP with Γ (circle centered at N(d,0) radius R). So E and F lie on line AP. So we have line AP, and points A, E, F on it (with A between? Possibly). So we can compute coordinates of E and F using the line equation.

We have A = (x_A, y_A). Parameterize line AP: A + t*(P - A). Then for t=0 we have A; t=1 we have P. We need E (on Ω, distinct from A) and F (on Γ, distinct from A). So we find t such that point lies on respective circle.

Let direction vector v = P - A = (p_x - x_A, -k - y_A). Let’s denote v = (v_x, v_y). Then point X(t) = (x_A + t v_x, y_A + t v_y). We know that at t=0, X=A is on both circles. For E, X(t) on Ω: |X(t) - M|^2 = r^2. M=(0,0). So (x_A + t v_x)^2 + (y_A + t v_y)^2 = r^2. Since A satisfies x_A^2 + y_A^2 = r^2, we have:

(x_A^2 + y_A^2) + 2t (x_A v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = r^2 => 2t (x_A v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0. So t=0 or t = -2 (x_A v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2. Thus t_E = -2 (x_A v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2.

Similarly, for F, X(t) on Γ: |X(t) - N|^2 = R^2. N=(d,0). So (x_A + t v_x - d)^2 + (y_A + t v_y)^2 = R^2. A satisfies (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2. Expand: (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 + 2t ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 => 2t ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0. So t=0 or t = -2 ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2. Thus t_F = -2 ((x_A - d) v_x + y_A v_y)/|v|^2.

Thus we have E = X(t_E), F = X(t_F). So coordinates expressed.

Let's try to compute using coordinates to see if we can prove tangency. Since we have explicit coordinates for A, P, H, and we can compute E and F in terms of A and P, then find circle (BEF) and verify that ℓ is tangent. This is algebraic but we can try to simplify using known relations.

Let’s define coordinates using parameters: d, r, R. But we also have x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d), y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). That's messy because of square root. So t_F = -2 ((x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - r^2 + x_A d)/|v|^2 = -2 ((x_A - d)p_x + y_A p_y - r^2 + x_A d)/|v|^2.

Let’s use the approach: Parameterize line AP with P as origin? Better: Use the fact that for any point X on line AP, we have power relation. Let’s define line AP: we have points A and P. Let’s set coordinate along line AP: let’s define a coordinate λ such that point X = P + λ * (A-P). Then λ=0 gives P, λ=1 gives A. Then the intersections with Ω satisfy: |X - M|^2 = r^2. Since A is on Ω, λ=1 is one solution. The other solution λ_E gives E. For a line through P, the product of signed distances from P to the intersection points with the circle equals the power of P with respect to the circle: (distance from P to A) * (distance from P to E) = Pow_Ω(P). But careful with signs: If we use signed distances along the direction from P to A, then PA = distance from P to A (positive). Let’s denote u = A-P. Then any point X = P + λ u. The condition |X-M|^2 = r^2 gives |P-M + λ u|^2 = r^2. Since A is on Ω, we have |P-M + u|^2 = r^2. So we have a quadratic in λ: |P-M|^2 + 2λ (P-M)·u + λ^2 |u|^2 = r^2. The constant term is |P-M|^2 - r^2 = -Pow_Ω(P) (since power = PM^2 - r^2). So the equation is λ^2 |u|^2 + 2λ (P-M)·u + (|P-M|^2 - r^2) = 0. The product of roots λ1 λ2 = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2 = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. One root is λ=1 (for A). So the other root λ_E satisfies 1 * λ_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2 => λ_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. But note: If we use signed distances, λ is the factor such that X = P + λ u; then the signed distance from P to X is λ * |u| (if we take direction u). So the product of signed distances is λ1 λ2 |u|^2 = -Pow_Ω(P). So indeed λ_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. So E = P + λ_E u, where u = A-P.

Similarly, for Γ, we have N: let’s define v = A-P (same u). Then intersection condition: |P - N + λ u|^2 = R^2. Since A on Γ, |P-N+u|^2 = R^2. The constant term is |P-N|^2 - R^2 = -Pow_Γ(P). So product of roots λ1 λ2 = (|P-N|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2 = -Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2. One root λ=1 gives A, so other root λ_F = -Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2. So F = P + λ_F u.

Thus we have: E = P - (Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2) u, and F = P - (Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2) u. Since u = A-P. So the points E and F lie on the line AP on the opposite side of P relative to A? The denominator: 2 sqrt( ((R+r)^2 - 1)(1 - (R-r)^2) ). Note that ((R+r)^2 - 1) = (R+r-1)(R+r+1). And (1 - (R-r)^2) = (1 - (R-r))(1+(R-r)) = (1 - (R-r))(1+(R-r)). But (1 - (R-r)) = (1 - (R-r)) = (1 - (R-r)) = (1 - (R-r)) and (1+R-r) = (1+R-r). Also (r+R+1) = (R+r+1). So the numerator is (1-(R-r)) (R+r+1) (1+R-r). So p_y = - ((1-(R-r))(R+r+1)(1+R-r))/(2 sqrt( (R+r-1)(R+r+1)(1-(R-r))(1+(R-r)) ). Let’s denote a = R-r, b = R+r. Then conditions: a < 1 < b, and r<R => a>0. Also 1-(R-r) = 1-a, and 1+R-r = 1+R-r = 1+(R-r) = 1+a. So numerator: (1-a)(b+1? Actually R+r+1 = b+1, and 1+R-r = 1+a. So numerator = (1-a)(b+1)(1+a). Denominator: 2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1)(1-a)(1+a) ) = 2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1)(1-a)(1+a) ). So p_y = - ((1-a)(1+a)(b+1))/(2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1)(1-a)(1+a) )) = - ( (1-a^2)(b+1) )/(2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1)(1-a^2) )) because (1-a)(1+a)=1-a^2. So p_y = - ( (1-a^2)(b+1) )/(2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1)(1-a^2) )) = - ( (1-a^2)(b+1) )/(2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1) ) sqrt(1-a^2) ) = - ( sqrt(1-a^2) (b+1) )/(2 sqrt( (b-1)(b+1) )). So p_y = - (b+1) sqrt(1-a^2) / (2 sqrt(b^2-1)). But b = R+r, a = R-r. So b^2 - 1 = (R+r)^2 - 1, and 1-a^2 = 1-(R-r)^2. So p_y = - (R+r+1) sqrt(1-(R-r)^2) / (2 sqrt((R+r)^2-1)). That's a nicer expression. And recall y_A = (1/2) sqrt( ((R+r)^2-1)(1-(R-r)^2) ). So we can also compute k = -p_y = (R+r+1) sqrt(1-(R-r)^2) / (2 sqrt((R+r)^2-1)). And p_x = (1+R-r)/2 = (1 + (R-r))/2? Actually 1+R-r = 1 + (R-r) = 1 + a. So p_x = (1+a)/2. So P = ( (1+a)/2, -k ).

Now H = (p_x, h_y) with h_y = p_x (1-p_x)/p_y. But p_y negative, so h_y negative. Compute p_x = (1+a)/2. Then 1-p_x = 1 - (1+a)/2 = (2 - 1 - a)/2 = (1 - a)/2. So p_x (1-p_x) = ((1+a)/2)*((1-a)/2) = (1 - a^2)/4. So h_y = (1 - a^2)/4 / p_y = (1 - a^2)/(4 p_y). Since p_y negative, h_y negative. But p_y = -k, so h_y = (1 - a^2)/(4 * (-k)) = -(1 - a^2)/(4k). So H = ( (1+a)/2, -(1-a^2)/(4k) ). And k = (R+r+1) sqrt(1-a^2)/(2 sqrt((R+r)^2-1)). So we can compute h_y in terms of a,R,r. But maybe we don't need explicit.

Now line ℓ: through H parallel to AP. AP direction: from A to P. We need to find the slope. A = (x_A, y_A). We have x_A = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2 = (1 - (R^2 - r^2))/2 = (1 - (R-r)(R+r))/2 = (1 - a b)/2. So x_A = (1 - a b)/2. And y_A = (1/2) sqrt( (b^2-1)(1-a^2) ). Since we have a = R-r, b = R+r.

Now P = ( (1+a)/2, -k ). So vector AP = ( (1+a)/2 - (1-ab)/2, -k - y_A ) = ( (1+a -1+ab)/2, -(k+y_A) ) = ( (a+ab)/2, -(k+y_A) ) = ( a(1+b)/2, -(k+y_A) ). So AP = (a(1+b)/2, -(k+y_A)). Since a>0, b>0. So slope m_AP = (-(k+y_A)) / (a(1+b)/2) = -2(k+y_A)/(a(1+b)). That is negative (since k,y_A>0). So ℓ: through H with same slope.

Now we need to show that ℓ is tangent to circle (BEF). Let's compute points E and F using the power method. We have u = A-P = - (P-A) = - (a(1+b)/2, -(k+y_A)) = (-a(1+b)/2, k+y_A). So u = (-a(1+b)/2, k+y_A). Then |u|^2 = (a^2 (1+b)^2)/4 + (k+y_A)^2. Also, we have Pow_Ω(P) = |P|^2 - r^2. But maybe we can find explicit coordinates for E and F. Since we have λ_E = - (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2, and λ_F = - (|P-1|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. So E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u.

Now the circle (BEF) has B = (x_A, -y_A). Since A and B are symmetric, B = conjugate of A. So B = x_A - i y_A in complex notation.

Given the complexity, maybe there is a known geometry result: In a configuration of two intersecting circles, the line through the orthocenter of triangle formed by the centers and the circumcenter of a certain triangle parallel to the line joining an intersection point and that circumcenter is tangent to the circumcircle of the other intersection and the two points where that line meets the circles again. We can attempt to prove the tangency condition using vectors or coordinates and simplify using algebraic identities. Let's proceed with analytic geometry using complex numbers with M=0, N=1, and treat points as complex numbers. We'll denote:

Let a = R - r (distance between radii), b = R + r. Then conditions: 0 < a < 1 < b, and r = (b-a)/2, R = (b+a)/2. But we also have that circles intersect: a < 1 < b.

Define:
- M = 0, N = 1.
- A is intersection of circles |z|=r and |z-1|=R. Since we choose upper intersection, we have A = x_A + i y_A with y_A > 0.
- Then B = conjugate of A = x_A - i y_A.
- C = -r (since C is on Ω on the ray from M away from N, so on negative real axis, distance r from M: C = -r).
- D = 1+R (since D is on Γ on ray from N away from M, so on positive real axis, distance R from N: D = 1+R).
- P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are real, P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD: x = (C+D)/2 = (-r + 1+R)/2 = (1+R-r)/2 = (1 + (R-r))/2 = (1+a)/2. So p_x = (1+a)/2. And p_y = - (x_A + r)(1+R-r)/(2 y_A) as derived. So P = p_x + i p_y, with p_y < 0.
- H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=0, N=1 are real, the altitude from P is vertical (perpendicular to MN which is horizontal), so H = (p_x, h_y) with h_y = p_x (1-p_x)/p_y (since p_y negative, h_y negative). So H = p_x + i h_y, with h_y = p_x (1-p_x)/p_y.
- Line AP: direction = A - P = (x_A - p_x) + i (y_A - p_y). Since p_y negative, y_A - p_y > 0.
- Points E and F: E is second intersection of line AP with Ω (|z|=r). Since A is on Ω, and line AP goes through P, we have by power of point P with respect to Ω: (PA)*(PE) = |P|^2 - r^2 (signed product). But careful with orientation: If we consider directed distances from P along line AP, then the product of the signed distances from P to A and P to E equals the power. Since we can treat complex numbers, we can find E = P + t_E (A-P) where t_E is a real number (not 0) such that |E|^2 = r^2. Using earlier parameterization: let u = A - P. Then points X = P + λ u. For λ=1 we get A, for λ=λ_E we get E. The condition |P + λ u|^2 = r^2 gives: |P|^2 + 2λ Re(P \bar{u}) + λ^2 |u|^2 = r^2. Since A satisfies |P+u|^2 = r^2, we have |P|^2 + 2 Re(P \bar{u}) + |u|^2 = r^2 => 2 Re(P \bar{u}) + |u|^2 = r^2 - |P|^2. So the quadratic becomes λ^2 |u|^2 + 2λ Re(P \bar{u}) + (|P|^2 - r^2) = 0. The product of roots λ1 λ2 = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. Since λ=1 is a root, the other root λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. But careful: The product of roots is constant term/leading coefficient? Actually equation: |u|^2 λ^2 + 2 Re(P \bar{u}) λ + (|P|^2 - r^2) = 0. The product of roots = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. So if λ1=1, then λ2 = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. So λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. Similarly, for Γ: let v = A-P, then condition |P-1+λ v|^2 = R^2 gives equation |v|^2 λ^2 + 2 Re((P-1) \bar{v}) λ + (|P-1|^2 - R^2)=0. Since λ=1 is root, the other root λ_F = (|P-1|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. So we have:

E = P + λ_E u, with λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2.
F = P + λ_F u, with λ_F = (|P-1|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2.

Note: Here we used u = A-P, so E = P + λ_E (A-P) = (1-λ_E)P + λ_E A. But λ_E is real (since it's a ratio of real numbers). So E and F are points on line AP.
