Given the difficulty, maybe we should attempt a coordinate proof with a clever simplification. Since we have explicit expressions in terms of a = R-r, b = R+r, and we know that y_A = (1/2) sqrt((b^2-1)(1-a^2)), x_A = (1 - a b)/2. Also p_x = (1+a)/2, k = (b+1) sqrt(1-a^2)/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)). And h_y = - (1-a^2)/(4k). So H = ( (1+a)/2, -(1-a^2)/(4k) ). Also u = A-P = (x_A - p_x, y_A - p_y) = ( (1-ab)/2 - (1+a)/2, y_A - (-k) ) = ( (1-ab -1 - a)/2, y_A + k ) = ( -(a+ab)/2, y_A+k ) = ( -a(1+b)/2, y_A+k ). So u = ( -a(1+b)/2, y_A+k ). Now y_A + k = (1/2) sqrt((b^2-1)(1-a^2)) + (b+1) sqrt(1-a^2)/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)) = ( sqrt(1-a^2)/2 ) [ sqrt(b^2-1) + (b+1)/sqrt(b^2-1) ] = ( sqrt(1-a^2)/2 ) [ ( (b^2-1) + (b+1) )/sqrt(b^2-1) ] = ( sqrt(1-a^2)/2 ) [ (b^2-1 + b+1)/sqrt(b^2-1) ] = ( sqrt(1-a^2)/2 ) [ (b^2+b)/sqrt(b^2-1) ] = ( sqrt(1-a^2)/2 ) * b(b+1)/sqrt(b^2-1). So y_A+k = (b(b+1) sqrt(1-a^2))/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)). Also note that a(1+b)/2 = a(b+1)/2. So u = ( -a(b+1)/2, b(b+1) sqrt(1-a^2)/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)) ). So u = (b+1)/2 * ( -a, b sqrt(1-a^2)/sqrt(b^2-1) ). So the direction vector of AP is u.

Now line ℓ: through H with direction u. So its parametric form: L(t) = H + t u. We need to show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). Let's compute points E and F in terms of these parameters.

We have λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. Compute |P|^2 = p_x^2 + k^2 = ((1+a)/2)^2 + k^2. Also r^2 =? We know r = (b-a)/2, so r^2 = (b-a)^2/4. So |P|^2 - r^2 = ((1+a)^2 - (b-a)^2)/4 + k^2 = [ (1+2a+a^2) - (b^2 -2ab + a^2) ]/4 + k^2 = [1+2a - b^2 + 2ab]/4 + k^2 = [1+2a(1+b) - b^2]/4 + k^2. But b = R+r, a = R-r, so b^2 = (R+r)^2 = R^2+2Rr+r^2, and a b = (R-r)(R+r)=R^2-r^2. So maybe simplify using that b^2 - 1 = (R+r)^2 - 1 and 1-a^2 = 1-(R-r)^2. But k^2 = (b+1)^2 (1-a^2) / (4(b^2-1)). So |P|^2 - r^2 = ((1+2a(1+b)-b^2)/4) + (b+1)^2 (1-a^2)/(4(b^2-1)). Factor 1/4: = 1/4 [ (1+2a(1+b)-b^2) + (b+1)^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) ]. But b^2-1 = (b-1)(b+1). So (b+1)^2/(b^2-1) = (b+1)^2/[(b-1)(b+1)] = (b+1)/(b-1). So |P|^2 - r^2 = 1/4 [ (1+2a(1+b)-b^2) + (b+1)(1-a^2)/(b-1) ]. This seems messy.

Maybe we can find simpler expressions for λ_E and λ_F by using power of point P with respect to the circles, but we also have that P is circumcenter of ACD, so PA = PC = PD. We might compute distances: PC = distance from P to C =? C = -r, P = (p_x, -k). So PC = sqrt( (p_x + r)^2 + k^2 ) = sqrt( ((1+a)/2 + r)^2 + k^2 ). But r = (b-a)/2, so p_x + r = (1+a)/2 + (b-a)/2 = (1+a+b-a)/2 = (1+b)/2. So PC = sqrt( ((1+b)/2)^2 + k^2 ) = sqrt( (1+b)^2/4 + k^2 ). And PA = distance from P to A =? A = (x_A, y_A). So PA = sqrt( (x_A - p_x)^2 + (y_A + k)^2 ) = sqrt( (x_A - (1+a)/2)^2 + (y_A + k)^2 ). But we also know that P is circumcenter, so PA = PC = PD. So we can use that to simplify.

Given the algebraic complexity, perhaps there is a more elegant synthetic approach. Let's think about the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on the x-axis, and P is below, H is the point such that PH is vertical and H lies on the line through M perpendicular to PN, etc. There is a known fact: The reflection of H across MN lies on the circumcircle of PMN. Also, note that the line through H parallel to AP: since AP is a line through A and P, and H is related to P, maybe we can show that H is the midpoint of the segment connecting the centers of the two circles? No.

Another idea: Use coordinates with complex numbers and compute the equation of circle (BEF) and show that the line L is tangent by verifying that the equation of L satisfies the condition that the power of any point on L with respect to circle (BEF) is zero? Actually for a line to be tangent, the distance from the center to the line equals the radius. So if we find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF and compute its distance to line L, and also compute the circumradius, we can show equality. We can try to compute O and radius using complex numbers. Since B = \bar{A}, and E and F are on line AP, we can perhaps find that the circle (BEF) is the reflection of the circle (A??) across the real axis? Not exactly.

Let's denote complex numbers: Let A = a, B = \bar{a}. Then E and F are on line through A and P. Let u = A - P. Then E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u, with λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2, λ_F = (|P-1|^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. Note that |P|^2 - r^2 = Power_Ω(P) = |P|^2 - r^2, and |P-1|^2 - R^2 = Power_Γ(P). Also, note that P is the circumcenter of ACD, so |P-A| = |P-C| = |P-D|. We might be able to express λ_E and λ_F in terms of something like distances to C and D? Because |P-C| = PA, and we might find that |P|^2 - r^2 = ? Using that C = -r, and P = p_x - i k? Actually C is real, so |P-C|^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + k^2 = ((1+b)/2)^2 + k^2. Maybe we can find that λ_E = - (some function) and λ_F = - (some function) such that E and F are related to the reflections of C and D? Let's try to compute E and F in terms of a,b. We have u = ( -a(b+1)/2, y_A+k ). But we also have that y_A = (1/2) sqrt((b^2-1)(1-a^2)). And k = (b+1) sqrt(1-a^2)/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)). So y_A+k = (b(b+1) sqrt(1-a^2))/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)). So u = ( -a(b+1)/2, b(b+1) sqrt(1-a^2)/(2 sqrt(b^2-1)) ). So u = (b+1)/2 * ( -a, b sqrt(1-a^2)/sqrt(b^2-1) ). So u is proportional to vector v = ( -a, b sqrt(1-a^2)/sqrt(b^2-1) ). Note that v has length: sqrt( a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) ) = sqrt( a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) ). Not particularly simple.

Now compute |u|^2 = ((b+1)^2/4) * ( a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) ). Also, compute |P|^2 - r^2. We have P = (p_x, -k) = ((1+a)/2, -k). So |P|^2 = ((1+a)/2)^2 + k^2 = (1+2a+a^2)/4 + k^2. And r^2 = (b-a)^2/4 = (b^2 - 2ab + a^2)/4. So |P|^2 - r^2 = [ (1+2a+a^2) - (b^2-2ab+a^2) ]/4 + k^2 = (1+2a - b^2 + 2ab)/4 + k^2. Now k^2 = (b+1)^2 (1-a^2) / (4(b^2-1)). So |P|^2 - r^2 = 1/4 [ (1+2a - b^2 + 2ab) + (b+1)^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) ] = 1/4 [ (1+2a - b^2 + 2ab) + (b+1)(1-a^2)/(b-1) ] because (b+1)^2/(b^2-1) = (b+1)/(b-1). So = 1/4 [ (1+2a - b^2 + 2ab) + (b+1)(1-a^2)/(b-1) ].

Now note that b^2 - 1 = (b-1)(b+1). So maybe we can combine terms over denominator (b-1). Let's compute:

Let T = (1+2a - b^2 + 2ab) + (b+1)(1-a^2)/(b-1). Write the first part as fraction with denominator (b-1): = [ (1+2a - b^2 + 2ab)(b-1) + (b+1)(1-a^2) ]/(b-1). Then λ_E = (|P|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2 = [1/4 * numerator/(b-1)] / [((b+1)^2/4) * ( a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) )] = [ numerator/(b-1)] / [ (b+1)^2 * ( a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) ) ].

But we also have a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) =? Let's compute:

a^2 + b^2 (1-a^2)/(b^2-1) = [ a^2 (b^2-1) + b^2 (1-a^2) ]/(b^2-1) = [ a^2 b^2 - a^2 + b^2 - a^2 b^2 ]/(b^2-1) = (b^2 - a^2)/(b^2-1) = ( (b-a)(b+a) )/(b^2-1) = ( (b-a)(b+a) )/(b^2-1). Then add (b+1)(1-a^2) = (b+1)(1-a^2). So total N = (b-1) -2a - b^3 + b^2 + 2ab^2 + (b+1)(1-a^2). Expand (b+1)(1-a^2) = (b+1) - (b+1)a^2 = b+1 - a^2 b - a^2. But note that we can also compute λ_E directly using geometry: Since E = second intersection of line AP with Ω, and P is the circumcenter of ACD, we might find that E is the reflection of C across the line through A and something? Given the complexity, maybe we can find a different coordinate system that simplifies the problem. Since the problem involves two circles intersecting, we can use a coordinate system where the radical axis is the y-axis, or where the line of centers is the x-axis. We already have that. Perhaps we can also use a coordinate system where the circle (ACD) is centered at the origin? That would simplify P=0. But then M and N are not at 0 and 1; they would be some points. So |M - C| = r, and M lies on line MN (x-axis). Similarly, |N - D| = R, and N on x-axis. And A is on both circles. So we have:

- Γ: center N on x-axis, radius R, passes through A and D. So |A-N|^2 = R^2, |D-N|^2 = R^2.

Also, since P=0 is circumcenter of ACD, we have A, C, D on circle radius R0. So |A|=|C|=|D|=R0.

We need to prove: The line through H (orthocenter of triangle PMN) parallel to AP (i.e., line through H parallel to A since P=0) is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. Now E is second intersection of line AP (i.e., line through origin and A) with Ω (circle centered at M, radius r). Similarly for Γ: t_F. So we have:

|A-M|^2 = (R0 cosθ - m)^2 + (R0 sinθ)^2 = R0^2 - 2m R0 cosθ + m^2 = r^2.
Similarly, |A-N|^2 = R0^2 - 2n R0 cosθ + n^2 = R^2.

Thus we have:

(1) R0^2 - 2m R0 cosθ + m^2 = r^2.
(2) R0^2 - 2n R0 cosθ + n^2 = R^2.

r = m + R0, R = R0 - n.

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a synthetic approach that is more elegant. Let's try to understand the configuration more deeply.

We have two circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. The line MN (centers) meets circles at C (on Ω) and D (on Γ). So C and D are the endpoints of the diameters? Now triangle ACD: points A (intersection), C (on Ω left), D (on Γ right). Its circumcenter P. So the circle (ACD) is the circumcircle of A, C, D. This circle might be orthogonal to something?

So M and N are on perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD respectively. So the triangle PMN: P is circumcenter of ACD, M is on perpendicular bisector of AC, N on perpendicular bisector of AD. So maybe triangle PMN is related to the triangle formed by the circumcenters of some triangles.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So PH is altitude to MN (horizontal). So H lies on vertical line through P. So H = (p_x, h_y) as before.

Now line through H parallel to AP: since AP is a line through A and P, and H is vertically below P (since h_y negative, p_y negative), the line through H parallel to AP is essentially the translation of AP by the vector from P to H. That is, if we take line AP and shift it so that P moves to H, we get a line through H parallel to AP. So L = AP + (H-P). So L is the line obtained by translating AP by vector v = H-P. Since H-P = (0, h_y - p_y) = (0, (h_y - p_y)). So L is the vertical translation of AP by some amount.

Now, we want to show that L is tangent to circle (BEF). Since E and F are on AP, the circle (BEF) passes through B and the two points on AP. So perhaps L is the tangent at B to circle (BEF)? (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2, (x_A - n)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2.

Subtracting: (x_A - m)^2 - (x_A - n)^2 = r^2 - R^2 => (x_A^2 -2mx_A+m^2) - (x_A^2 -2nx_A+n^2) = -2m x_A + m^2 +2n x_A - n^2 = 2x_A(n-m) + (m^2-n^2) = 2x_A(n-m) + (m-n)(m+n) = (n-m)(2x_A - (m+n)). So (n-m)(2x_A - (m+n)) = r^2 - R^2. Since n>m, we have 2x_A - (m+n) = (r^2 - R^2)/(n-m). But we can also find x_A and y_A directly.

Also, note that the circumcenter of triangle ACD: C=(-c,0), D=(c,0), A=(x_A,y_A). Since C and D are symmetric about y-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is the y-axis (x=0). So P lies on y-axis: P = (0, p_y). And we have PA = PC = PD. So we can compute p_y from condition PA = PC:

PA^2 = (x_A - 0)^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2 = x_A^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2.
PC^2 = ( -c - 0 )^2 + (0 - p_y)^2 = c^2 + p_y^2.
Set equal: x_A^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2 = c^2 + p_y^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 - 2y_A p_y + p_y^2 = c^2 + p_y^2 => x_A^2 + y_A^2 - 2y_A p_y = c^2 => 2y_A p_y = x_A^2 + y_A^2 - c^2. But note that x_A^2 + y_A^2 =? Also, we have m = r-c, so m^2 = (r-c)^2 = r^2 -2rc + c^2. Then x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2 + 2(r-c)x_A - (r^2 -2rc + c^2) = 2(r-c)x_A + 2rc - c^2. So not constant. But we can compute p_y = (x_A^2 + y_A^2 - c^2)/(2y_A). That is similar to earlier.

So λ_E = (m^2+p_y^2 - r^2)/|u|^2, λ_F = (n^2+p_y^2 - R^2)/|u|^2. But we also have that r = m+c, and R = c-n. So m^2+p_y^2 - r^2 = m^2+p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 = m^2+p_y^2 - m^2 -2mc - c^2 = p_y^2 -2mc - c^2. Now also note that we have relation from A being on Ω: |A-M|^2 = r^2. That gives (x_A - m)^2 + y_A^2 = (m+c)^2. Given the complexity, perhaps we should try to compute the condition using coordinates with m,n,c as parameters, and then simplify using the expressions for x_A, y_A, p_y, etc. We'll compute the circle (BEF) and then compute the distance from H to L? The condition for a line through H to be tangent to a circle is that the line is tangent, which is equivalent to that the point H lies on the polar of the point of tangency, but we don't know the tangency point.

Better: The line L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to L equals the circumradius R_BCF. Since we have parametric line L(t) = H + t u, the foot of perpendicular from O to L is some point; the condition is that the quadratic in t for intersection has discriminant zero. So if we find the circle equation in coordinates, we can compute the intersection condition. That is algebraic but we can attempt to simplify using the relations we have.

Let's proceed with coordinates: set x-axis = MN, y-axis = perpendicular bisector of CD (through P). So we have:

- M = (m, 0) with m < 0.
- N = (n, 0) with n > 0.
- C = (-c, 0), D = (c, 0), with c > 0, and -c < m < 0 < n < c, and n - m < c.
- A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A > 0, and x_A = c (m+n)/(n-m).
- B = (x_A, -y_A).
- P = (0, p_y), where p_y = m (x_A + c)/y_A.
- H = (0, h_y) = (0, - n y_A/(x_A+c)).

Now, we also have u = A - P = (x_A, y_A - p_y). So L(t) = (t x_A, h_y + t (y_A - p_y)).

Now, we need to compute E and F. We have:

|u|^2 = x_A^2 + (y_A - p_y)^2.
λ_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2, λ_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2cn)/|u|^2.

Then E = (λ_E x_A, p_y + λ_E (y_A - p_y)).
F = (λ_F x_A, p_y + λ_F (y_A - p_y)).

Now, note that p_y and y_A are related. Also, note that we can compute y_A - p_y = y_A - m(x_A+c)/y_A = (y_A^2 - m(x_A+c))/y_A. And we have y_A^2 = 2m x_A + 2mc + c^2 - x_A^2. So y_A^2 - m(x_A+c) = 2m x_A + 2mc + c^2 - x_A^2 - m x_A - m c = m x_A + m c + c^2 - x_A^2 = m(x_A + c) + c^2 - x_A^2.

Thus y_A - p_y = (m(x_A+c) + c^2 - x_A^2)/y_A.

Now, also note that x_A = c (m+n)/(n-m). So we can compute x_A + c = c ( (m+n)/(n-m) + 1 ) = c ( (m+n)+(n-m) )/(n-m) = c (2n)/(n-m) = 2c n/(n-m). So x_A + c = 2c n/(n-m). Similarly, x_A - c = c ( (m+n)/(n-m) - 1 ) = c ( (m+n)-(n-m) )/(n-m) = c (2m)/(n-m) = 2c m/(n-m). Since m<0, x_A - c is negative, consistent.

Thus we have:

x_A + c = 2c n / s, where s = n-m >0.
x_A - c = 2c m / s.

For a triangle, the Simson line of a point on the circumcircle is the line containing the feet of perpendiculars from that point to the sides. We can use that to simplify y_A^2 expression. Let's compute y_A^2 directly from the circle equations using our parameters. Now also p_y = m (x_A + c)/y_A = m * (c(2m+1)+c)/y_A = m * (2c(m+1))/y_A = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. So p_y = 2c m(m+1)/y_A. Since m(m+1) is negative, p_y negative.

Now we can compute u = (x_A, y_A - p_y) = (c(2m+1), y_A - 2c m(m+1)/y_A) = (c(2m+1), (y_A^2 - 2c m(m+1))/y_A). But y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)(-m(m+1)). Now we need to prove it generally. Given the parameterization with m and c, we have:

- m ∈ (-1,0), c>1.

- y_A = sqrt(4c(c-1)(-m(m+1))). We can set y_A = 2 sqrt(c(c-1)(-m(m+1))). But we can also keep y_A^2.

So M is not at origin; we have translation. To set M at origin, we would translate horizontally by -m. That would change the midpoint of CD. So we have two different coordinate systems. In the numeric example, we used the parameterization with m and c and then computed points; we didn't set M at origin. We used the coordinate system where midpoint of CD is origin. So we have:

- M = (m,0), N = (m+1,0), C = (-c,0), D = (c,0).
- A = (x_A, y_A) with x_A = c(2m+1), y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)(-m(m+1)).

Now, the circle (BEF). Since B = (x_A, -y_A). And E and F lie on line through P with direction u. So we can try to find the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Perhaps we can find its equation in a nice form using complex numbers. Let's use complex numbers with the same coordinate system. Let’s denote:

- Let’s set the complex plane with origin at the midpoint of CD (which is the y-axis). Then:

Now, we need to find the circle through B = x_A - i y_A, E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u. Since P = i p_y, and λ_E, λ_F are real.

We want to prove that the line through H parallel to u is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the line L: H + t u (t real) is tangent to that circle. - λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2. Compute (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2. And p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). So λ_E = (cα/(c-1) - (m^2+2mc+c^2))/|u|^2.
- λ_F = (c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2)/|u|^2 = (c(2m+2-c) + cα/(c-1))/|u|^2 = c(2m+2-c + α/(c-1))/|u|^2.

Now, we can also express m^2 in terms of α: m^2 = -α - m. So (m^2+2mc+c^2) = -α - m + 2mc + c^2 = c^2 + 2mc - m - α. So λ_E = (cα/(c-1) - (c^2+2mc-m-α))/|u|^2 = (cα/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m + α)/|u|^2 = (α (c/(c-1) + 1) - c^2 - 2mc + m)/|u|^2 = (α (c/(c-1) + (c-1)/(c-1))? Actually α * (c/(c-1) + 1) = α * (c/(c-1) + (c-1)/(c-1)) = α * (2c-1)/(c-1). So λ_E = (α(2c-1)/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m)/|u|^2. But note that α = -m(m+1). So α(2c-1)/(c-1) = -m(m+1)(2c-1)/(c-1). And -c^2 -2mc + m = -c^2 -2mc + m. So λ_E = ( -m(m+1)(2c-1)/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m )/|u|^2. That is not obviously factorable.

Similarly, λ_F = c(2m+2-c + α/(c-1))/|u|^2 = c(2m+2-c + (-m(m+1))/(c-1))/|u|^2. So λ_F = c(2m+2-c - m(m+1)/(c-1))/|u|^2.

Now, maybe we can find λ_E and λ_F such that E and F are related to the reflections of C and D? Let's compute the coordinates of E and F in terms of m,c. Since we have u = x_A + i k y_A, and we have P = i p_y, so E = i p_y + λ_E u. So E = (λ_E x_A, p_y + λ_E k y_A). Similarly, F = (λ_F x_A, p_y + λ_F k y_A). And B = (x_A, -y_A). So we have three points.

Given the time, I think we should produce a coordinate solution that is rigorous and complete. I'll do it with a systematic approach.

I'll set up coordinate system: Let MN be the x-axis, with M at (0,0), N at (d,0), where d>0. Since the radius of Ω is less than that of Γ, we have r<R. The circles intersect at two points, so d^2 < (R+r)^2 and d^2 > (R-r)^2. Also, the line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C-M-N-D. Since M is at (0,0), C is on the ray from M opposite N, so C = (-r, 0). Since N is at (d,0), D is on the ray from N away from M, so D = (d+R, 0). So we have:

C = (-r, 0), M = (0,0), N = (d,0), D = (d+R, 0).

Now, let A be the upper intersection of the circles. Then A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A>0, and:

x_A^2 + y_A^2 = r^2, (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2.

Subtracting: (x_A - d)^2 - x_A^2 = R^2 - r^2 => -2d x_A + d^2 = R^2 - r^2 => x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). Then y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). So we have explicit.

Now, P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line at x = (C+D)/2 = (-r + d+R)/2 = (d+R-r)/2. So p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Then p_y is determined by PA = PC. PC^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + p_y^2. PA^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2. Set equal:

(p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + p_y^2.

We can solve for p_y. Expanding: (p_x - x_A)^2 + p_y^2 - 2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + p_y^2 => cancel p_y^2: (p_x - x_A)^2 - 2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2. So 2p_y y_A = (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)/2)^2. Then p_y = [ (p_x - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)/2)^2 ] / (2 y_A). We can compute this explicitly. But we already derived a nicer expression: p_y = - (x_A + r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A). Let's verify: Since p_x = (d+R-r)/2, we have p_x - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - x_A. And we had earlier p_y = - (x_A + r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A). This should be correct if we use the relation from the circle equations. Let's derive quickly: Using the fact that A lies on Ω, we have x_A^2+y_A^2=r^2. Also, from the condition that P is circumcenter, we have |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2. This gave: (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 => p_x^2 -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 + p_y^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = p_x^2 +2p_x r + r^2 + p_y^2 => -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = 2p_x r + r^2 => x_A^2+y_A^2 -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x r + r^2 => r^2 -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x r + r^2 => -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2p_x r => divide 2: -p_x x_A - p_y y_A = p_x r => p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A + r). So p_y = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A. Since p_x = (d+R-r)/2, we get p_y = - (x_A+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A). Yes. So that's correct.

Thus P = ( (d+R-r)/2, - (x_A+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y_A) ).

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=(0,0), N=(d,0) are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical line x = p_x, so H lies on x = p_x. Let H = (p_x, h_y). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (d - p_x, 0 - p_y) = (d - p_x, -p_y). Since M=(0,0), the line MH is perpendicular to PN, so (p_x, h_y) dot (d - p_x, -p_y) = 0 => p_x (d - p_x) - h_y p_y = 0 => h_y = p_x (d - p_x)/p_y. Since p_y negative, h_y is negative if p_x(d-p_x)>0. So H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y). That matches our earlier.

Now, line AP: direction u = A - P = (x_A - p_x, y_A - p_y). Since P is on vertical line, we can compute.

Points E and F: second intersections of line AP with circles Ω and Γ. We can compute using power. For Ω: Let λ be such that point X = P + λ u lies on Ω. Then |X - M|^2 = r^2. Since A = P + u, we have λ=1 gives A. So the quadratic in λ: |P - M + λ u|^2 = r^2 => |P-M|^2 + 2λ Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + λ^2 |u|^2 = r^2. But A satisfies: |P-M+u|^2 = r^2 => |P-M|^2 + 2 Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + |u|^2 = r^2. So the quadratic can be written as: λ^2 |u|^2 + 2λ Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + (|P-M|^2 - r^2) = 0. The constant term is |P-M|^2 - r^2 = -Pow_Ω(P). The product of roots = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2 = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. Since one root is λ=1, the other root λ_E = -Pow_Ω(P)/|u|^2. Similarly, for Γ: λ_F = -Pow_Γ(P)/|u|^2. So E = P + λ_E u, F = P + λ_F u.

Now, we need to prove that the line through H parallel to u is tangent to circle (BEF). Since we have all points in terms of d,r,R (or equivalently d, r, R), we can attempt to compute the condition.

Given the time, I think I will produce a solution that uses coordinates but with a clever normalization that reduces the algebra. I'll use the coordinate system where the midpoint of CD is origin and x-axis is MN. Then we have parameters m (negative) and c>1. We derived expressions for points. Now we can compute the circle (BEF) by using the fact that the circumcircle of three points can be found by solving for the circle equation. Since we have symbolic expressions, we can attempt to compute the center O. Let's try to compute O in terms of m and c.

We have:

B = (x_A, -y_A) = (c(2m+1), -y_A)
E = (λ_E x_A, p_y + λ_E k y_A)
F = (λ_F x_A, p_y + λ_F k y_A)

We can find the circumcenter O = (X,Y) by solving the linear equations from the equalities of distances. Since B and E and F are not symmetric, but we can compute using the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates. Alternatively, we can use the fact that the circle through B and two points on line AP can be described by the equation that the power of any point on AP is constant. Since E and F are on AP, the line AP is the chord of the circle (BEF) that passes through B? Actually, B is not on AP generally. So the circle (BEF) has chord EF on line AP. The perpendicular bisector of EF is a line perpendicular to AP at the midpoint of EF. That line is not necessarily vertical. The circumcenter O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BF (or BE). So we can find O by solving two linear equations.

Let’s compute the coordinates explicitly using the expressions we have. We have:

x_A = c(2m+1)
y_A = 2√(c(c-1)α) with α = -m(m+1). But we can also keep y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α.

We also have:

p_y = -2cα/y_A = -c√α/√(c(c-1)). That's negative.

k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1))

Now compute λ_E and λ_F. We have:

λ_E = (p_y^2 - (m+c)^2)/|u|^2
λ_F = (c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2)/|u|^2

But we also have |u|^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 y_A^2. And p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). And (m+c)^2 = m^2+2mc+c^2.

We can try to simplify λ_E and λ_F using the relations between m and α. Since α = -m(m+1), we have m^2 = -α - m. So (m+c)^2 = -α - m + 2mc + c^2 = c^2 + 2mc - m - α.

Thus p_y^2 - (m+c)^2 = cα/(c-1) - (c^2+2mc - m - α) = cα/(c-1) - c^2 - 2mc + m + α.

Similarly, for λ_F: c(2m+2-c) + p_y^2 = 2cm + 2c - c^2 + cα/(c-1).

Now, we also have expressions for x_A and y_A in terms of m,c. x_A = c(2m+1). So x_A^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2. y_A^2 = 4c(c-1)α.

First, compute t_E and t_F more explicitly. We have:

But we also have α = -m(m+1). So α/(c-1) = -m(m+1)/(c-1). So t_E = ( -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2mc - c^2 )/|u|^2 = - ( m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2mc + c^2 )/|u|^2.

Similarly, t_F = ( -m(m+1)/(c-1) - c^2 + 2c(m+1) )/|u|^2 = ( -m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2c(m+1) - c^2 )/|u|^2.

Now, note that 2c(m+1) - c^2 = 2c m + 2c - c^2. So t_F = ( -m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2c m + 2c - c^2 )/|u|^2.

Now, also note that we can write |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + cα (2c-1)^2/(c-1). Since α = -m(m+1), we have cα = -c m(m+1). So |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 - c m(m+1) (2c-1)^2/(c-1). That is |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 - c m(m+1) (2c-1)^2/(c-1).

Thus t_E = - ( m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2mc + c^2 ) / |u|^2. Since denominator is positive, t_E negative.

Now, we also have p_y = -2cα/Y = 2c m(m+1)/Y? Actually p_y = 2c m(m+1)/Y (since α = -m(m+1), so -2cα = 2c m(m+1)). So p_y = 2c m(m+1)/Y. Since m(m+1) negative, p_y negative.

Now, we also have k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). So kY = (2c-1)Y/(2(c-1)). And Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, so Y = 2 sqrt(c(c-1)α). That is messy.

Maybe we can find a nicer expression by using the fact that the line AP is the line through P and A. Since we have P=(0,p_y) and A=(x_A,Y) with Y positive, the line AP has equation. Perhaps we can find the coordinates of E and F by solving the intersection with circles directly, rather than using t_E. That might lead to simpler expressions. Let's do that.

Circle Ω: x^2 + y^2 = r^2. Since A is on it, and we have line AP: parametric from P to A. Let's find the equation of line AP. The vector from P to A is (x_A, Y-p_y). So param: (0,p_y) + t (x_A, Y-p_y). For t=1, we get A. The other intersection E corresponds to some t_E. Plug into circle: (t x_A)^2 + (p_y + t (Y-p_y))^2 = r^2. This is a quadratic in t. Since t=1 is a root, we can find t_E by Vieta: the sum of roots = -2 Re((P-M) \bar{u})/|u|^2? Actually we can compute directly: The quadratic is: |u|^2 t^2 + 2 Re((P-M) \bar{u}) t + (|P-M|^2 - r^2) = 0. Since t=1 is a root, we have: |u|^2 + 2 Re((P-M) \bar{u}) + (|P-M|^2 - r^2) = 0. Then the other root t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. So t_E = (|P-M|^2 - r^2)/|u|^2. That's our t_E.

Now, we can compute |P-M|^2 - r^2 = (m^2 + p_y^2) - (m+c)^2 = p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2. So t_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: |P-N|^2 - R^2 = ((m+1)^2 + p_y^2) - (c-(m+1))^2 = p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1). So t_F = (p_y^2 - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2.

Now, we can compute p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). And 2mc + c^2 = c(2m+c). So t_E = (cα/(c-1) - c(2m+c))/|u|^2 = c(α/(c-1) - (2m+c))/|u|^2. And α = -m(m+1). So α/(c-1) - (2m+c) = -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2m - c = - [ m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m + c ]. So t_E = -c[ m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m + c ]/|u|^2.

Similarly, t_F = (cα/(c-1) - c^2 + 2c(m+1))/|u|^2 = c(α/(c-1) - c + 2(m+1))/|u|^2 = c( -m(m+1)/(c-1) - c + 2m+2 )/|u|^2 = c( -m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m+2 - c )/|u|^2 = c( -m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2(m+1) - c )/|u|^2 = c( (m+1)(2 - m/(c-1)) - c )/|u|^2? Not simple.

But note that we have relation from the circle Ω: (x_A - m)^2 + Y^2 = (m+c)^2. Since x_A = c(2m+1), we can compute (x_A - m)^2 = (c(2m+1)-m)^2 = (2c m + c - m)^2 = ((2c-1)m + c)^2. So ((2c-1)m + c)^2 + Y^2 = (m+c)^2. So Y^2 = (m+c)^2 - ((2c-1)m + c)^2. Expand: (m^2+2mc+c^2) - ((2c-1)^2 m^2 + 2c(2c-1)m + c^2) = m^2+2mc+c^2 - (2c-1)^2 m^2 - 2c(2c-1)m - c^2 = m^2(1-(2c-1)^2) + 2m(c - c(2c-1)) = m^2(1-4c^2+4c-1) + 2m(c - 2c^2 + c) = m^2(-4c^2+4c) + 2m(2c - 2c^2) = 4c m^2(1-c) + 4c m(1-c) = 4c(1-c)(m^2+m) = 4c(1-c)α. Since α = -m(m+1), we have 4c(1-c)α = 4c(1-c)(-m(m+1)) = 4c(c-1)m(m+1)? Actually 1-c = -(c-1), so 4c(1-c)α = 4c * (-(c-1)) * (-m(m+1)) = 4c(c-1)m(m+1). But we had Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α? Wait, we defined α = -m(m+1), so m(m+1) = -α, so 4c(c-1)m(m+1) = -4c(c-1)α. That gives Y^2 = -4c(c-1)α, which is positive because α>0? Actually α = -m(m+1) >0, so -4c(c-1)α is negative, which is impossible. So there is a sign mistake. Let's recalc: Y^2 = (m+c)^2 - ((2c-1)m + c)^2. We computed that as 4c(1-c)(m^2+m). Since 1-c is negative (c>1), and m^2+m is negative (m∈(-1,0)), so product positive. So Y^2 = 4c(1-c)(m^2+m) = 4c * (negative) * (negative) = positive. So Y^2 = 4c (c-1) (-m^2-m)? Actually 1-c = -(c-1), so 4c(1-c)(m^2+m) = -4c(c-1)(m^2+m). Since m^2+m = m(m+1) = -α, so -4c(c-1)(-α) = 4c(c-1)α. So Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α. That's consistent with earlier Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α. Good. So Y = 2√(c(c-1)α). So Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α. Good.

Now, p_y = m(x_A+c)/Y = m(2c(m+1))/Y = 2c m(m+1)/Y = -2cα/Y. So p_y = -2cα/Y.

Now, compute t_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. We have p_y^2 = 4c^2α^2/Y^2 = 4c^2α^2/(4c(c-1)α) = cα/(c-1). So p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). And 2mc + c^2 = c(2m+c). So t_E = (cα/(c-1) - c(2m+c))/|u|^2 = c(α/(c-1) - (2m+c))/|u|^2. Now α/(c-1) = -m(m+1)/(c-1). So α/(c-1) - (2m+c) = -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2m - c = -[ m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m + c ]. So t_E = -c[ m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m + c ]/|u|^2.

Now, we can also compute |u|^2 = x_A^2 + (Y-p_y)^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + (Y - (-2cα/Y))^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + (Y + 2cα/Y)^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + Y^2 + 4cα + 4c^2α^2/Y^2? Actually (Y+2cα/Y)^2 = Y^2 + 4cα + 4c^2α^2/Y^2. So |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + Y^2 + 4cα + 4c^2α^2/Y^2. But Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, so |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + 4c(c-1)α + 4cα + 4c^2α^2/(4c(c-1)α) = c^2(2m+1)^2 + 4c^2α + 4c^2α/(c-1)? Wait: 4c(c-1)α+4cα = 4cα((c-1)+1)=4c^2α. Then 4c^2α^2/Y^2 = 4c^2α^2/(4c(c-1)α)= cα/(c-1). So |u|^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2 + 4c^2α + cα/(c-1). That's messy.

Given the difficulty, I think I will present a solution that uses coordinates and presents the algebraic verification in a structured way. Since we have already reduced the problem to verifying that the distance from O to line L equals the radius, we can compute O by solving the linear equations. Let's do that.

We have B=(x_A, -Y), E=(t_E x_A, p_y + t_E k Y), F=(t_F x_A, p_y + t_F k Y). Let's denote:

Let’s use the following: Since the points are not symmetric, but we can find the circumcenter O by using the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates: O = (X, Y_O) where X = ( (|B|^2 (E-F) + |E|^2 (F-B) + |F|^2 (B-E) ) / (2 (B × E + E × F + F × B) )? Actually, there is a formula using the fact that the circumcenter is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors. We can compute using the fact that the circumcenter is the point that is equidistant to B,E,F. So we have:

|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 => (X-x_A)^2 + (Y_O+Y)^2 = (X - t_E x_A)^2 + (Y_O - (p_y + t_E k Y))^2.

Expanding: X^2 -2X x_A + x_A^2 + Y_O^2 + 2Y_O Y + Y^2 = X^2 -2X t_E x_A + t_E^2 x_A^2 + Y_O^2 -2Y_O(p_y + t_E k Y) + (p_y + t_E k Y)^2.

Cancel X^2, Y_O^2: -2X x_A + x_A^2 + 2Y_O Y + Y^2 = -2X t_E x_A + t_E^2 x_A^2 -2Y_O(p_y + t_E k Y) + (p_y + t_E k Y)^2.

Bring terms: -2X x_A + 2X t_E x_A + 2Y_O Y + 2Y_O(p_y + t_E k Y) + x_A^2 + Y^2 - t_E^2 x_A^2 - (p_y + t_E k Y)^2 = 0 => 2X x_A (t_E -1) + 2Y_O (Y + p_y + t_E k Y) + (x_A^2(1-t_E^2) + Y^2 - (p_y + t_E k Y)^2) = 0.

Thus we have linear equation: 2x_A (t_E-1) X + 2(Y + p_y + t_E k Y) Y_O + C_E = 0, where C_E = x_A^2(1-t_E^2) + Y^2 - (p_y + t_E k Y)^2.

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2: 2x_A (t_F-1) X + 2(Y + p_y + t_F k Y) Y_O + C_F = 0, with C_F = x_A^2(1-t_F^2) + Y^2 - (p_y + t_F k Y)^2.

So we can solve for X and Y_O. This is doable if we compute these coefficients.

Given the symbolic complexity, but we can try to simplify using the relations. Note that Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, p_y = -2cα/Y, and k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). Also, t_E and t_F are expressed in terms of m,c. Now, we need to show that the distance from O to line L equals the radius. L: through H=(0,-Y/(2c)) with direction u=(x_A, kY). In numeric, H=(0,-0.2828). u=(1.2,1.697). The line L: (0,-0.2828)+t(1.2,1.697). The distance from O to L is |(O-H) × u| / |u|. Compute O-H = (0.298, -1.777+0.2828) = (0.298, -1.4942). Now, to prove it generally, we need to show that the distance from O to L equals the radius. That is:

|(O-H) × u| / |u| = |O-B|.

This is equivalent to |(O-H) × u| = |u| * |O-B|.

E = (t_E x_A, p_y + t_E k Y)
F = (t_F x_A, p_y + t_F k Y)

We want to find real numbers A,B,C such that:

|P|^2 + A X + B Y + C = 0 for P = B,E,F.

So we have:

(1) x_A^2 + Y^2 + A x_A + B (-Y) + C = 0.
(2) (t_E x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_E k Y)^2 + A (t_E x_A) + B (p_y + t_E k Y) + C = 0.
(3) (t_F x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_F k Y)^2 + A (t_F x_A) + B (p_y + t_F k Y) + C = 0.

Subtract (1) from (2) and (3) to eliminate C. This gives two linear equations in A and B. Then we can solve for A and B. Then C can be found from (1). Then we can compute the quadratic in t when substituting L(t) = (t x_A, h_y + t k Y). That is:

(t x_A)^2 + (h_y + t k Y)^2 + A (t x_A) + B (h_y + t k Y) + C = 0.

This is a quadratic in t. The discriminant must be zero. This is a condition that we can verify using the relations between the parameters. This is a systematic approach, but the algebra is heavy. Actually, the image of any point under a spiral similarity centered at A is on the ray from A through the point rotated by θ. So C' is on the ray from A through C rotated by θ. That ray is not horizontal. So the image of C is not on the horizontal line through N. So something is wrong: The spiral similarity centered at A that sends M to N sends the line through M and N (which is MN) to a line through N that is the image of MN under the transformation. Since the transformation is a spiral similarity (rotation+scaling) about A, it sends any line not through A to a line parallel to it? Actually, a spiral similarity is a composition of rotation and scaling about A. It sends any line L to a line L' that is the image of L under the rotation+scaling. If L does not pass through A, then L' is not necessarily parallel to L; it is rotated by the same rotation angle. For a line L that does not pass through A, its image L' is a line that is rotated by the angle θ relative to L, but not necessarily parallel. So the image of MN under the spiral similarity is a line through N that is rotated by θ relative to MN. Since MN is horizontal, the image is a line through N at angle θ. So the image of C is on that line. So my earlier statement that the image of line MN is a line through N parallel to MN is false for a spiral similarity; it's true for a homothety (scaling) about A, but not for a rotation+scaling unless the rotation is 0. So the spiral similarity is not a homothety; it rotates as well. So the image of MN is a line through N that makes angle θ with MN. So the image of C lies on that line. So not on x-axis.

Thus that approach is not.

Now, compute t_E = (p_y^2 - 2mc - c^2)/|u|^2. We have p_y^2 = cα/(c-1). And 2mc + c^2 = c(2m+c). So t_E = (cα/(c-1) - c(2m+c))/|u|^2 = c(α/(c-1) - (2m+c))/|u|^2. Now α = -m(m+1). So α/(c-1) - (2m+c) = -m(m+1)/(c-1) - 2m - c = -[ m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m + c ]. So t_E = -c[ m(m+1)/(c-1) + 2m + c ]/|u|^2.

Let’s compute t_E k Y. Since k Y = u_y, we have t_E u_y. So p_y + t_E u_y = p_y + t_E u_y. Similarly, p_y + t_F u_y.

Now, we need to find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Instead of solving linear equations, we can use the following approach: The circumcenter O is the point that is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of EF is the line through the midpoint of EF perpendicular to u. Let's compute the midpoint of EF: M_EF = ( (t_E + t_F)x_A/2, (p_y + t_E u_y + p_y + t_F u_y)/2 ) = ( (t_E+t_F)x_A/2, p_y + (t_E+t_F)u_y/2 ). So M_EF = ( (t_E+t_F)x_A/2, p_y + (t_E+t_F)u_y/2 ). The perpendicular direction to u is v = (-u_y, x_A). So the perpendicular bisector of EF is: (X,Y) = M_EF + s v, where s ∈ ℝ.

Now, the perpendicular bisector of BF: B=(x_A, -Y), F=(t_F x_A, p_y + t_F u_y). Midpoint of BF: ( (x_A + t_F x_A)/2, (-Y + p_y + t_F u_y)/2 ) = ( x_A(1+t_F)/2, (p_y - Y + t_F u_y)/2 ). The direction BF = F-B = ( (t_F-1)x_A, p_y + t_F u_y + Y ). A perpendicular direction to BF is w = ( -(p_y + t_F u_y + Y), (t_F-1)x_A ). So the perpendicular bisector of BF is: (X,Y) = ( x_A(1+t_F)/2, (p_y - Y + t_F u_y)/2 ) + t w.

Now, O is the intersection of these two lines. So we have:

M_EF + s v = ( x_A(1+t_F)/2, (p_y - Y + t_F u_y)/2 ) + t w.

This gives two equations. Since v and w are not parallel, we can solve for s and t. Then we can find O. This is still messy.

Given the time, I think I will produce a solution that uses coordinates and then uses the fact that the circle (BEF) can be expressed by the equation:

|z|^2 + A x + B y + C = 0, and then compute the discriminant when substituting L. This is a linear system in A,B,C. Since we have three points, we can solve for A,B,C. Then we can compute the condition for tangency by plugging L into the circle equation and showing the discriminant is zero. This is a systematic approach that can be done with symbolic algebra. I'll try to do that now.

Let’s denote:

B = (x_A, -Y)
E = (t_E x_A, p_y + t_E u_y)
F = (t_F x_A, p_y + t_F u_y)

We want to find real numbers A,B,C such that:

x_A^2 + Y^2 + A x_A + B (-Y) + C = 0   (1)
(t_E x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 + A (t_E x_A) + B (p_y + t_E u_y) + C = 0   (2)
(t_F x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_F u_y)^2 + A (t_F x_A) + B (p_y + t_F u_y) + C = 0   (3)

Subtract (1) from (2) and (3) to eliminate C. We get:

(2)-(1): [t_E^2 x_A^2 - x_A^2] + [(p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2)] + A x_A (t_E-1) + B (p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0.

But careful: (2) minus (1) gives: (t_E^2 x_A^2 - x_A^2) + (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) + A(t_E x_A - x_A) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0. Wait, (1) had term +C, (2) had +C, so they cancel. So we have:

(t_E^2 x_A^2 - x_A^2) + (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) + A x_A (t_E-1) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0. But note that (2) also had term +C, (1) had +C, so they cancel. So yes.

Thus:

x_A^2(t_E^2 -1) + (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) + A x_A (t_E-1) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0.   (EqE)

Similarly, (3)-(1): x_A^2(t_F^2 -1) + (p_y + t_F u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) + A x_A (t_F-1) + B(p_y + t_F u_y + Y) = 0.   (EqF)

Now, note that x_A^2+Y^2 = r^2? Actually from circle Ω: x_A^2+Y^2 = r^2. So (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) = (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - r^2.

Also, we know that for E on Ω, we have |P + t_E u|^2 = r^2. But P = (0,p_y), u = (x_A, u_y). So |P + t_E u|^2 = (t_E x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 = r^2. So (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 = r^2 - t_E^2 x_A^2. So then (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) = r^2 - t_E^2 x_A^2 - r^2 = - t_E^2 x_A^2. So (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) = - t_E^2 x_A^2. Similarly, for F: (p_y + t_F u_y)^2 - (x_A^2+Y^2) = - t_F^2 x_A^2.

Thus EqE becomes:

x_A^2(t_E^2 -1) - t_E^2 x_A^2 + A x_A (t_E-1) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0 => x_A^2(t_E^2 -1 - t_E^2) + A x_A (t_E-1) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0 => x_A^2(-1) + A x_A (t_E-1) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0 => -x_A^2 + A x_A (t_E-1) + B(p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = 0.   (1)

Similarly, EqF: -x_A^2 + A x_A (t_F-1) + B(p_y + t_F u_y + Y) = 0.   (2)

Thus we have two linear equations in A and B:

A x_A (t_E-1) + B (p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = x_A^2.   (1')
A x_A (t_F-1) + B (p_y + t_F u_y + Y) = x_A^2.   (2')

So the coefficients of A and B are different. Let’s denote:

Let’s compute p_y + t_E u_y + Y. Since u_y = kY, and p_y = -2cα/Y. But we can also compute using the fact that E lies on Ω: (t_E x_A)^2 + (p_y + t_E u_y)^2 = r^2. But we already used that. Maybe we can find a simpler expression for p_y + t_E u_y + Y. Since p_y = -2cα/Y, and u_y = kY, we have p_y + t_E u_y = -2cα/Y + t_E kY. Then adding Y gives -2cα/Y + t_E kY + Y. This is not obviously factorable.

But we can also use the fact that B, E, F are concyclic, so the coefficients A,B,C are uniquely determined. We can solve for A and B by subtracting the two equations:

x_A (t_E-1) A + (p_y + t_E u_y + Y) B = x_A^2
x_A (t_F-1) A + (p_y + t_F u_y + Y) B = x_A^2

Subtract: x_A (t_E - t_F) A + [ (p_y + t_E u_y + Y) - (p_y + t_F u_y + Y) ] B = 0 => x_A (t_E - t_F) A + (t_E - t_F) u_y B = 0 => (t_E - t_F)( x_A A + u_y B ) = 0. Since t_E ≠ t_F (generally), we get:

x_A A + u_y B = 0.   (3)

So B = - (x_A / u_y) A.

Now, plug into (1'):

A x_A (t_E-1) + (- (x_A / u_y) A) (p_y + t_E u_y + Y) = x_A^2 => A x_A [ (t_E-1) - (p_y + t_E u_y + Y)/u_y ] = x_A^2 => A [ (t_E-1) - (p_y + t_E u_y + Y)/u_y ] = x_A.

Thus:

A = x_A / [ (t_E-1) - (p_y + t_E u_y + Y)/u_y ].

Simplify denominator: (t_E-1) - (p_y + t_E u_y + Y)/u_y = (t_E-1) - (p_y + Y)/u_y - t_E.

So = (t_E-1 - t_E) - (p_y + Y)/u_y = -1 - (p_y + Y)/u_y.

Thus A = x_A / [ -1 - (p_y + Y)/u_y ] = x_A / ( - (1 + (p_y+Y)/u_y) ) ) = - x_A / ( 1 + (p_y+Y)/u_y ).

Thus A = - x_A / (1 + (p_y+Y)/u_y). Similarly, B = - (x_A / u_y) A = - (x_A / u_y) * ( - x_A / (1 + (p_y+Y)/u_y) ) = x_A^2 / ( u_y (1 + (p_y+Y)/u_y) ) = x_A^2 / ( u_y + p_y + Y ). So B = x_A^2 / (u_y + p_y + Y). And A = - x_A / (1 + (p_y+Y)/u_y) = - x_A * u_y / (u_y + p_y + Y) = - x_A u_y / (u_y + p_y + Y). So we have:

A = - x_A u_y / (u_y + p_y + Y)
B = x_A^2 / (u_y + p_y + Y)

Now, we also have C from (1): x_A^2 + Y^2 + A x_A + B (-Y) + C = 0 => C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - A x_A + B Y.

Thus the circle equation is:

X^2 + Y^2 + A X + B Y + C = 0.

Now, the line L: (x,y) = (t x_A, h_y + t u_y), where h_y = -Y/(2c). So we substitute:

(t x_A)^2 + (h_y + t u_y)^2 + A (t x_A) + B (h_y + t u_y) + C = 0.

This is a quadratic in t. The discriminant must be zero for tangency. Let's compute the coefficients.

Let’s denote:

X = t x_A, Y = h_y + t u_y.

Then:

X^2+Y^2 = t^2 x_A^2 + (h_y^2 + 2h_y u_y t + u_y^2 t^2) = t^2 (x_A^2+u_y^2) + 2h_y u_y t + h_y^2.

Then the circle equation becomes:

t^2 (x_A^2+u_y^2) + 2h_y u_y t + h_y^2 + A x_A t + B (h_y + t u_y) + C = 0 => t^2 (x_A^2+u_y^2) + (2h_y u_y + A x_A + B u_y) t + (h_y^2 + B h_y + C) = 0.

Thus the quadratic is: α t^2 + β t + γ = 0, with α = x_A^2+u_y^2, β = 2h_y u_y + A x_A + B u_y, γ = h_y^2 + B h_y + C.

We need to show that the discriminant D = β^2 - 4αγ = 0.

Now, we have expressions for A and B in terms of x_A, u_y, Y, p_y. And we have h_y = -Y/(2c). Also, we have relations between these parameters: x_A = c(2m+1), u_y = kY, p_y = -2cα/Y, and Y = 2√(c(c-1)α). So we can compute everything in terms of m and c. This is still heavy, but maybe we can find that β and α,γ are such that D=0 identically.

We can try to compute β and γ using the definitions. Since we have A and B expressed, we can also compute C. But perhaps there is a simpler way: Since the circle passes through B, E, F, and we have the line L through H parallel to AP, we can consider the power of H with respect to the circle. The power of H is |H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C. If the line L is tangent, then H lies on the polar of the point at infinity? Not.

But we can also compute the condition that L is tangent if and only if the equation for t has a double root. This is equivalent to that the line L is the radical axis of the circle (BEF) and the circle with diameter something? Not.

Since we have already done a numeric verification, we are confident. We can try to derive the discriminant identity symbolically.

Let’s compute the necessary quantities in terms of m and c. We have:

- x_A = c(2m+1)
- Y = 2√(c(c-1)α) with α = -m(m+1)

- p_y = -2cα/Y = -c√α/√(c(c-1))
- h_y = -Y/(2c)

Now, compute u_y + p_y + Y. Since u_y = kY, and p_y = -2cα/Y. So u_y + p_y + Y = Y(1+k) - 2cα/Y. But Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, so α/Y = α / (2√(c(c-1)α)) = √α/(2√(c(c-1))). So 2cα/Y = 2c * (α/Y) = 2c * (√α/(2√(c(c-1)))) = c√α/√(c(c-1)). That's not obviously related.

Maybe we can compute u_y + p_y + Y in a simpler form. Note that u_y = Y - p_y? Actually from earlier, u_y = Y - p_y? Wait, we have u = (x_A, u_y) and p_y is the y-coordinate of P. We derived u_y = Y - p_y? Actually A=(x_A,Y), P=(0,p_y), so u_y = Y - p_y. But we also have u_y = kY. So kY = Y - p_y => p_y = Y - kY = Y(1-k). So p_y = Y(1-k). And we also have p_y = -2cα/Y. So Y(1-k) = -2cα/Y => Y^2(1-k) = -2cα. But Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, so 4c(c-1)α(1-k) = -2cα => divide cα (nonzero): 4(c-1)(1-k) = -2 => 2(c-1)(1-k) = -1 => (c-1)(1-k) = -1/2. Now compute k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). Then 1-k = 1 - (2c-1)/(2(c-1)) = (2(c-1) - (2c-1))/(2(c-1)) = (2c-2-2c+1)/(2(c-1)) = (-1)/(2(c-1)). So (c-1)(1-k) = (c-1)*(-1)/(2(c-1)) = -1/2. Yes, that holds. So consistent.

Now, u_y + p_y + Y = u_y + Y + p_y = kY + Y + p_y = Y(1+k) + p_y. But p_y = Y(1-k), so = Y(1+k+1-k) = 2Y. So u_y + p_y + Y = 2Y! That's a nice simplification. Indeed, u_y + p_y + Y = kY + Y + (Y - kY) = 2Y. So the denominator simplifies to 2Y.

Thus we have:

A = - x_A u_y / (u_y + p_y + Y) = - x_A u_y / (2Y) = - x_A (u_y)/(2Y).
But u_y = kY, so A = - x_A kY/(2Y) = - (x_A k)/2.

Similarly, B = x_A^2 / (u_y + p_y + Y) = x_A^2 / (2Y) = x_A^2/(2Y).

So we have simple expressions:

A = - (x_A k)/2
B = x_A^2/(2Y)

Now, we also need C. From (1): C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - A x_A + B Y. Plug A and B:

C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - (- (x_A k)/2) x_A + (x_A^2/(2Y)) Y = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 k)/2 + (x_A^2)/2 = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2.

So C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2.

Now, we have the circle equation:

X^2 + Y^2 + A X + B Y + C = 0.

Now, the line L: (x,y) = (t x_A, h_y + t u_y) = (t x_A, -Y/(2c) + t k Y). So param t.

Now, compute α = x_A^2+u_y^2 = x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2. But we also have Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α, and x_A^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2. So α = c^2(2m+1)^2 + k^2 * 4c(c-1)α? Wait, careful: u_y = kY, so u_y^2 = k^2 Y^2. So α = x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2.

Now, β = 2h_y u_y + A x_A + B u_y. Compute:

2h_y u_y = 2 * (-Y/(2c)) * (kY) = - (kY^2)/c.
A x_A = (- (x_A k)/2) * x_A = - (x_A^2 k)/2.
B u_y = (x_A^2/(2Y)) * (kY) = (x_A^2 k)/2.

So A x_A + B u_y = - (x_A^2 k)/2 + (x_A^2 k)/2 = 0. So β = 2h_y u_y = - (kY^2)/c.

Thus β = - (k Y^2)/c.

Now, γ = h_y^2 + B h_y + C. Compute:

h_y^2 = Y^2/(4c^2).
B h_y = (x_A^2/(2Y)) * (-Y/(2c)) = - (x_A^2)/(4c).
C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2.

So γ = Y^2/(4c^2) - x_A^2/(4c) - x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2 = combine terms: Y^2 terms: Y^2/(4c^2) - Y^2 = Y^2(1/(4c^2) - 1) = Y^2( (1 - 4c^2)/(4c^2) ). x_A^2 terms: - x_A^2/(4c) - x_A^2 + (x_A^2 (k+1))/2 = x_A^2( -1/(4c) - 1 + (k+1)/2 ) = x_A^2( -1/(4c) - 1 + k/2 + 1/2 ) = x_A^2( k/2 - 1/(4c) - 1/2 ). So γ = Y^2(1-4c^2)/(4c^2) + x_A^2( k/2 - 1/(4c) - 1/2 ).

Now, we need to show that D = β^2 - 4αγ = 0.

Compute β^2 = (k^2 Y^4)/c^2.

Now, α = x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2.

Thus 4αγ = 4(x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2) * [ Y^2(1-4c^2)/(4c^2) + x_A^2( k/2 - 1/(4c) - 1/2 ) ].

Let’s compute step by step.

First, note that Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α? Actually we have α (the parameter) = -m(m+1). But we already used Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α. So Y^2 = 4c(c-1)α. And x_A^2 = c^2(2m+1)^2. Also, k = (2c-1)/(2(c-1)). So we can express everything in terms of m and c. This is still heavy.

Given the time, I think we can produce a solution that uses the algebraic simplifications we have already done, and then compute D and show it is zero using the relations. Let's try to compute γ in a nicer form using the circle equation that passes through B. Since B lies on the circle, we have:

x_A^2 + Y^2 + A x_A + B (-Y) + C = 0 => C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - A x_A + B Y.

We already used that. Maybe we can find an expression for γ directly in terms of the line L and the circle. Another approach: The line L is tangent to the circle if and only if the power of H with respect to the circle is equal to the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency? Not.

But we can compute the power of H: Pow_H = |H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C. Since H = (0, -Y/(2c)). So H_x=0, H_y=-Y/(2c). So Pow_H = (Y^2/(4c^2)) + A*0 + B*(-Y/(2c)) + C = Y^2/(4c^2) - (B Y)/(2c) + C.

Now, if the line L through H is tangent, then H lies on the tangent line, and the foot of perpendicular from O to L is the point of tangency T. Then |O-H|^2 = R^2 + (HT)^2? Actually, if a line through H is tangent to a circle, then the distance from H to the point of tangency is the length of the tangent segment from H to the circle. That tangent length squared is Pow_H (since power of H relative to circle is the square of the length of the tangent from H to the circle if H is outside the circle). So if H is outside the circle, then the tangent length is sqrt(Pow_H). But the line L is a line through H; if it is tangent, then the distance from H to the point of tangency is exactly the tangent length. But the point of tangency T is not necessarily the foot of perpendicular from O to L? Actually, for a line through H that is tangent to a circle, the point of tangency T is the point on the line where the line touches the circle. Then the segment HT is the tangent segment. The distance from O to the line is the perpendicular distance from O to the line, which is the distance from O to T (since T is the foot of perpendicular from O to the line because the radius to the point of tangency is perpendicular to the tangent). So indeed, the distance from O to L is the length of the radius, and the foot is T. So we have |O-T| = R, and T is the projection of O onto L. Also, HT is the distance along L from H to T. Then by the Pythagorean theorem, |O-H|^2 = |O-T|^2 + |H-T|^2 = R^2 + (HT)^2. So |O-H|^2 - R^2 = (HT)^2 = Pow_H. So the condition for tangency is that the power of H equals the difference of squares of distances from O to H and from O to center? Actually, we have: Pow_H = |H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C = (HT)^2. Also, |O-H|^2 = R^2 + (HT)^2. So |O-H|^2 = R^2 + Pow_H. That is always true if H is outside the circle? Actually, if H is outside, then the tangent length squared is Pow_H. And for any point H, the power is the square of the tangent length from H to the circle. So if a line through H is tangent, then the distance from H to the point of tangency is sqrt(Pow_H). But also, the distance from O to the line is R. So we can derive a relation: The distance from O to L is R. That is the condition. But we can also compute |O-H|^2 - R^2 = (distance from H to line)^2? Actually, if L is a line through H, then the distance from H to the line is 0. So not.

Thus we can't use that directly.

Given the time, I think we have a workable algebraic proof that we have simplified to showing that the quadratic in t has discriminant zero. We have expressions for α, β, γ in terms of x_A, Y, k, c. Now, we can use the relations between x_A, Y, k, c to compute D. Since we already have that A = - (x_A k)/2, B = x_A^2/(2Y), and that h_y = -Y/(2c), we can compute γ using the circle equation (1) and the fact that B lies on the circle. But we already have γ = h_y^2 + B h_y + C. And we can compute C from (1): C = -x_A^2 - Y^2 - A x_A + B Y. So γ = h_y^2 + B h_y - x_A^2 - Y^2 - A x_A + B Y.

Now, we have A x_A = - (x_A k)/2 * x_A = - x_A^2 k/2. B Y = (x_A^2/(2Y))*Y = x_A^2/2. So γ = h_y^2 + B h_y - x_A^2 - Y^2 + (x_A^2 k)/2 + x_A^2/2 = h_y^2 + B h_y - x_A^2 - Y^2 + x_A^2 (k+1)/2.

Now, h_y^2 = Y^2/(4c^2). B h_y = (x_A^2/(2Y))*(-Y/(2c)) = - x_A^2/(4c). So γ = Y^2/(4c^2) - x_A^2/(4c) - x_A^2 - Y^2 + x_A^2 (k+1)/2 = combine Y^2: Y^2/(4c^2) - Y^2 = Y^2(1/(4c^2)-1) = Y^2( (1-4c^2)/(4c^2) ). Combine x_A^2: - x_A^2/(4c) - x_A^2 + x_A^2 (k+1)/2 = x_A^2( -1/(4c) - 1 + (k+1)/2 ) = x_A^2( (k+1)/2 - 1 - 1/(4c) ). So γ = Y^2(1-4c^2)/(4c^2) + x_A^2( (k+1)/2 - 1 - 1/(4c) ).

Now, we need to compute α = x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2.

So 4αγ = 4(x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2) * [ Y^2(1-4c^2)/(4c^2) + x_A^2( (k+1)/2 - 1 - 1/(4c) ) ] = (x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2) * [ Y^2(1-4c^2)/c^2 + 4x_A^2( (k+1)/2 - 1 - 1/(4c) ) ].

Now, β^2 = k^2 Y^4 / c^2.

So D = k^2 Y^4 / c^2 - (x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2) * [ Y^2(1-4c^2)/c^2 + 4x_A^2( (k+1)/2 - 1 - 1/(4c) ) ].

Factor 1/c^2: D = (1/c^2)[ k^2 Y^4 - (x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2) * Y^2(1-4c^2) - (x_A^2 + k^2 Y^2) * 4x_A^2( (k+1)/2 - 1 - 1/(4c) ) ].

Next, x_A^2 Y^2(1-4c^2) = x_A^2 * 4c(c-1)α * (1-4c^2) = 4c(c-1)α x_A^2 (1-4c^2). So that's fine.

Now, k^2 Y^2 = k^2 * 4c(c-1)α = 4c(c-1)α k^2. So x_A^2 k^2 Y^2/(c(c-1)) = x_A^2 * (4c(c-1)α k^2)/(c(c-1)) = 4α x_A^2 k^2. So third term = x_A^4/(c(c-1)) + 4α x_A^2 k^2.

Now, distance from O to line L. L: through H = (0, -s/5) = (0, -0.28284) with direction u = (6/5, 6s/5) = (1.2, 1.697055). The distance from O to L is |(O-H) × u| / |u|.

Compute O-H = (3/10 - 0, -5s/4 + s/5) = (0.3, s(-5/4 + 1/5)) = (0.3, s(-25/20+4/20)) = (0.3, s*(-21/20)) = (0.3, -21s/20). So O-H = (3/10, -21s/20). u = (6/5, 6s/5) = (1.2, 6s/5). Cross product magnitude: | (3/10)*(6s/5) - (-21s/20)*(6/5) | = | (18s/50) + (126s/100) | = | (36s/100 + 126s/100) | = | (162s/100) | = (162s)/100 = (81s)/50. So |cross| = (81√2)/50 ≈ 81*1.4142/50=114.55/50=2.291. |u| = sqrt( (6/5)^2 + (6s/5)^2 ) = sqrt(36/25 + 72/25) = sqrt(108/25) = (√108)/5 = (6√3)/5 ≈ 6*1.732/5=10.392/5=2.0784. So distance = (81√2/50) / (6√3/5) = (81√2/50)*(5/(6√3)) = (81√2*5)/(50*6√3) = (405√2)/(300√3) = (27√2)/(20√3) = (27√6)/60 = (9√6)/20 ≈ 1.102. So distance = 9√6/20 = R. So indeed, L is tangent. So the algebra works out with the exact values.

Thus we have a rigorous algebraic proof: using the coordinate system with midpoint of CD as origin, x-axis = MN, we can derive that the circumcenter O of triangle BEF is (3/10, -5√2/4) and the line through H parallel to AP is tangent. However, this is for the specific numeric values? No, we used m=-1/5, c=2 as an example. For a general m and c, the algebra should hold similarly. So we need to prove that for any m∈(-1,0) and c>max(m+1, -m), the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of BEF. The algebraic simplification we did for the numeric case suggests that the general proof would involve showing that the distance from O to L equals the radius. We can attempt to compute O and the distance in general.

We can use the fact that the circle (BEF) is the circumcircle of triangle BEF. There is a formula for the circumcenter in terms of coordinates: For points B=(x1,y1), E=(x2,y2), F=(x3,y3), the circumcenter is given by:

X = ( (x1^2+y1^2)(y2-y3) + (x2^2+y2^2)(y3-y1) + (x3^2+y3^2)(y1-y2) ) / ( 2( x1(y2-y3) + x2(y3-y1) + x3(y1-y2) ) )
Y = ( (x1^2+y1^2)(x3-x2) + (x2^2+y2^2)(x1-x3) + (x3^2+y3^2)(x2-x1) ) / ( 2( x1(y2-y3) + x2(y3-y1) + x3(y1-y2) ) )
